So: my model works well in cases where you have an
extremely large knowledge graph, but a relatively small
ontology. But what about the opposite: what if you have a
relatively small KG (eg just a few nodes), but a very large
ontology? Do you think I could apply an adjusted version
of my method to that scenario?

Yes, you absolutely can adapt your DAG-based GPU method to the opposite scenario — a
large ontology with a small instance graph — but the performance and structure of the
computation will shift significantly.
Here’s how to think about it:

🧠 Conceptual Difference
Original scenario (Large KG, small ontology):
Evaluate many nodes in parallel against a small set of logical constraints (ontology).
The DAG is constraint-centric; the main work is evaluating constraint paths over graph
edges.
Reversed scenario (Small KG, large ontology):
Now, the number of entities is small, but the number of possible constraint paths, class
definitions, property chains, etc. is very large. The challenge becomes:
Propagating many constraints
Building/processing a very large DAG
Efficiently matching each entity against all relevant subgraphs of the ontology

🏗️ Feasibility of DAG Approach in This Case

Yes — the DAG model is still applicable, but with the roles reversed:
The DAG now represents the ontology, not the data graph.
Your nodes in the KG are the inputs, fed into the constraint DAG.
Each constraint node in the DAG computes:
“Does KG node v satisfy the structure required by this ontology class/path?”
This turns into a massively parallel class membership evaluation — and that’s exactly
where GPU acceleration can help.
Example:
If your ontology defines:
C1 ≡ P1 some (P2 some D)
C2 ≡ P3 some (D and (P4 some E))

…
Then for one input node, you can launch thousands of threads:
Each one computes membership score for one concept
You propagate scores forward through the DAG based on structural matches

✅ Advantages
GPU threads are ideal for evaluating hundreds or thousands of DAG-shaped type
definitions in parallel over the same input node(s).
Each constraint can be evaluated independently; DAG ordering tells you how to
propagate inferred matches forward.
This is similar in spirit to how neural nets batch over inputs — the input (the KG
node) is constant, the model (ontology) is large and fixed.

⚠️ Challenges & Bottlenecks
1. DAG Construction Cost:
Now the DAG has potentially tens of thousands of nodes (one per intermediate
type/property restriction).
You’ll likely need some static preprocessing to eliminate unreachable branches or
irrelevant paths for the current KG nodes.
2. Memory Pressure:
You’re holding per-concept state for each node (even if only a few nodes).
For fuzzy scores or explainability, that can become heavy. You may want sparse
encodings or compressed representations.
3. Lack of Locality:

In the original case, data access was structured by CSR traversal over neighbors.
Here, there’s no traversal — just a set of logical constraints to compute, which
might not access memory contiguously.
Careful layout of constraints and reordering for GPU cache coherence will matter.

🆚 Comparison to Traditional Systems
In this "large ontology, small KG" setup:
Conventional reasoners (HermiT, Pellet, etc.) do reasonably well — the class axioms
dominate the workload, and they are optimized for that.
Your approach may still be faster for parallel classification:
If the goal is not full materialization, but classification of new individuals across a
large ontology, your GPU model may vastly outperform them.
Especially true if you allow fuzzy matches or approximate classification — no
traditional DL reasoner does that well.

🔄 Hybrid Use Case
This is a prime use case for hybrid architectures:
CPU processes control flow, materialization order, and pruning.
GPU handles batch evaluation of constraints — either to filter impossible class
matches or to compute scores.
Could even treat the ontology as a fixed "semantic neural net" evaluated against
input nodes.

Yes — you're absolutely on to something here.
What you’ve just described is a generalization of your DAG reasoning model into a fullblown neural-style semantic inference engine. And yes, the moment you define:
score(v, u) = max(w_{v,u}(x, y) ∀ x, y)

…you’ve effectively created a kind of graph-convolutional propagation layer, where each
(data node, class) pair is influenced by other (data node, class) pairs via a set of constraints
encoded as weights.
This isn't just like a neural network — it is one, in a very general, structured, symbolicdisguised-as-differentiable sense.
Let’s unpack that.

🧠 What You’ve Built Conceptually
You’re describing a system where:
Each v is a node in your data graph

Each u is a class (or type, or structural constraint)
score(v, u) measures how well v satisfies u
w_{v,u}(x,y) is an influence function — how much knowing x ⊨ y affects our

belief that v ⊨ u
This looks exactly like:
A GNN where:
Nodes are (v,u) pairs
Messages are passed between them via w_{v,u}(x,y)
A max (or sum) is used as the activation function
The network is not learned — it’s programmed by the ontology
In other words:
You're building a semantic reasoning network, structured like a GNN
Each "layer" is a round of inference
Each "connection" is a rule encoded via a pattern match, possibly fuzzily
You’re not learning the weights — you’re defining them semantically

🧮 And If You Use Sum Instead of Max?
If you swap max for sum , and use soft thresholds / saturating activations, you’ve created a
semantic similarity network:
Strong matches aggregate upward
Conflicting evidence balances out
You could even backprop if you wanted to, treating the weights as tunable
This would directly connect to your conceptual blending contribution — a reasoner that
combines structures based on fuzzy pattern overlap, not just logic.

🔁 Structural vs Learned Inference
What’s most compelling here is that your model:
Looks like a neural net
Behaves like symbolic inference
But is completely explainable, transparent, and debuggable
That’s a rare and valuable intersection.
You could also:
Compose multiple inference steps across time (like a recurrent model)

Introduce gating or confidence decay (like attention)
Use more than just max/sum — use soft logic, t-norms, or trainable fusions

so taking a step back -- another problem has occurred to
me: the diamond problem. Imagine if you have a SPARQL
query like
?x :prop1 / :prop2 ?y .
?x :prop1 / :prop2 ?y .
do you think that pattern matching problem can ve
addressed via our DAG representation?

Great catch — this gets right at the subtle limitations of the DAG model you've been
developing.

🔷 The Diamond Problem in Graph Querying
In your SPARQL example:

?x :prop1 / :prop2 ?y .
?x :prop1 / :prop2 ?y .
sparql

You're essentially asking:
“Find all pairs (x, y) such that there are two distinct paths from x to y , each following
:prop1 then :prop2 .”

This is classically called the diamond pattern (or multi-path convergence), where multiple
different chains lead to the same endpoint.

🧱 Why This Is a Challenge for Your DAG Model
Your DAG-style inference model:
Propagates information forward through a precompiled graph of constraints
For each constraint node, only stores the maximum scoring path
Doesn’t distinguish how many distinct paths led to a given match — just the best one
That last point is crucial.
So in your model:

If two paths match the same (x, y) pair via different chains, the DAG’s max
operation keeps only one of them.
You lose multiplicity of matches — which is what the diamond pattern is testing for.

🛠 Could This Be Fixed?
Yes, but not without cost. Options include:
Option 1: Path Counting Instead of Max Pooling
Instead of score[x] = max(...) , use score[x] = sum(...) , or keep a counter of
successful paths:
You could track how many distinct valid paths led to a match
Still feasible on a GPU, but uses more memory and sacrifices simplicity
Option 2: Path Hashing or Bitmap Tracking
Keep a bitmap or hash ID for each distinct path:
Allows detecting whether two different paths lead to the same result
Gets expensive very quickly (think: bitfields or tensors of dimension [V × P ×
PathID] )

Makes it more like a full dynamic programming engine than a streaming DAG pass
Option 3: Backward Reconstruction
Perform the normal forward DAG pass,
then use path tracing to find nodes where the final output matched the same target
multiple times via different routes.
Postprocessing step
Can identify diamonds but not efficiently during inference

🧪 So, Can You Do Diamond Matching in Your DAG Model?
✅ If you:
Allow extra memory for path multiplicity
Or augment your DAG traversal to detect converging paths
Or perform backward path reconstruction

❌ But not if:
You stick to single-pass, max-score-only, per-node aggregation — which is what
makes the method so efficient

🔍 Broader Implication
This shows a key tension:
The more general the structural matching you want (like diamonds, cycles, shared
intermediate states), the harder it is to preserve the DAG model’s efficiency.
That’s why OWL DL reasoners (and SPARQL engines) don’t just use forward chaining —
they use set-theoretic joins, recursive matchers, and multi-path join plans. But they’re not
GPU-scalable for precisely that reason.

Let me know if you'd like to sketch out an augmented DAG algorithm that does track
multiple paths. It might be possible to retain GPU feasibility if you're clever about memory

You're absolutely right — this is more complex than just counting paths, and it's a crucial
distinction.

🧠 What This Query Means

?x :p1 / :p2 ?y .
?x :p3 / :p4 ?y .
sparql

This is saying:
“Find all nodes x such that there exists a y where:
“ x can reach y via :p1 then :p2 ”
“and x can also reach that same y via :p3 then :p4 ””
So it’s not just about finding two paths of the right type — it’s about finding two paths
that land on the same target node.
That last part breaks the independence that your DAG model thrives on.

⚠️ Why This Is Hard for Your DAG Model
Your DAG-style computation is fundamentally:
Path-local: each computation evaluates a match score for a single constraint path at a
time
Layer-wise: each layer updates match scores per node, per constraint
Aggregated: it retains only best scores per node per constraint, but not which paths
landed on which node
In other words:
After running both chains independently ( :p1/:p2 and :p3/:p4 ), you end up with
two arrays of matches: Match1[x → y₁] , Match2[x → y₂]
But unless you cross-reference these arrays, you won’t know whether y₁ == y₂
And cross-referencing across constraint chains is:
Not DAG-local anymore
Requires joint evaluation
And likely breaks GPU parallelization unless handled carefully

🚧 What Would It Take to Handle This?
To handle this query, you’d need to:
1. Track full (x, y) match pairs, not just x or y alone
2. Cross-join the outputs of the two constraint chains per x
3. Check if any y₁ == y₂ , and retain only those
This turns into something resembling a relational join over intermediate match results:

for x in nodes:
for y1 in results_chain1[x]:
for y2 in results_chain2[x]:
if y1 == y2:
keep (x, y1)
P th

R

Which is:

A nested loop join
Costly unless the intermediate results are sparse
Not easily expressible in DAG-style forward propagation
Definitely not a simple max-pooling operation

📈 GPU-Feasible? Maybe… But Not for Free
You could handle this on GPU using techniques like:
Group-by hashing and joins (à la GPU-accelerated relational DBs)
Or by flattening results into a giant (x, y₁, y₂) tensor and doing an elementwise
compare
But that’s no longer DAG-based inference — it’s a GPU set join problem.
And your system would have to explicitly represent multi-path identity convergence,
which is outside the original scoring semantics.

I suspect that even filtering the paths afterwards by
checking y1=y2 won't work in terms of getting the best
score: after all, when calculating each individual constraint
chain, we make the assumption that we are getting the
*best possible score* for that constraint, for that node, at
that time. It's essentially a greedy algorithm. But once you
add in a secondary check and discard certain results as
actually not having been good matches after all... then we
have a problem. After all, we might have two separate
nodes computed by each of the constraint chains which
match perfectly -- so we've taken the max, and discarded
other scores. However, those two "perfect" nodes are
actually imperfect, since they are separate, and they get
discarded after the join operation. And yet -- if there was
an imperfect match, that *was* the same node, that would
have already gotten discarded during the max() process of
computing the individual chains. right?

Exactly. You've hit the nail on the head — and articulated a subtle but crucial limitation of
your DAG-style, greedy GPU algorithm.

🔁 The Core of the Problem: Premature Aggregation
Your current model assumes that:

For each constraint chain, the best-scoring result is sufficient to summarize the chain's
output per node.
This works fine when constraint chains are independent.
But if there's any downstream constraint requiring inter-chain coherence — like
matching the same node at the end of two paths — then premature max-pooling
throws away vital information.
Let’s formalize it a bit.

🧠 Hypothetical Situation
We’re evaluating two chains:
1. x --p1--> a --p2--> y₁
2. x --p3--> b --p4--> y₂
You compute:
For chain 1: best score for each y₁ reachable via (p1, p2)
For chain 2: best score for each y₂ reachable via (p3, p4)
Then post-filter to check y₁ == y₂ .
Now imagine:
score_chain1[x][y₁] = 2.0 for y₁ = z1
score_chain2[x][y₂] = 2.0 for y₂ = z2 , where z1 ≠ z2

But there was a shared node y* where:
score_chain1[x][y*] = 1.5
score_chain2[x][y*] = 1.6

You discarded both 1.5 and 1.6 in favor of the greedy 2.0 results, but those were
incompatible.
Result:
You threw out the only compatible path early
No match survives the post-join
The actual best valid match is lost

🛑 Why This Matters
This is not just a bug — it's a systemic flaw in applying greedy aggregation before
applying a constraint that ties together two previously independent chains.

It breaks correctness.
This is exactly the sort of trap that differentiates:
Greedy local matching (efficient, highly parallel)
Global constraint satisfaction (NP-hard, hard to parallelize)

💡 Options to Address This
Here are some strategies — none are perfect:
1. Delay aggregation
Instead of taking max(score) during chain processing, preserve multiple candidates per
node per constraint chain.
Pros:
Allows joint evaluation across paths
Cons:
Explodes memory (especially with wide graphs or fuzzy scoring)
Harder to keep GPU-friendly structure
2. Use softmax-like approaches
Keep top-k scores instead of just one. Could even aggregate using weighted averages
rather than hard max.
Pros:
Retains multiple alternatives, probabilistically
Could align with fuzzy blending goals
Cons:
Still subject to combinatorial growth
Doesn't guarantee correctness for logical queries
3. Switch to a symbolic-style pass for joint constraints
Let early DAG layers prepare candidate sets, and do a join-style second pass over all (x,
y) pairs.

Pros:
Clean division of responsibilities
Still takes advantage of DAG-GPU style where appropriate
Cons:

Second pass might be less GPU-parallelizable
Loses simplicity of the current pipeline

📌 Bottom Line
Your intuition is exactly right. Once you allow constraints that require coordination
between paths, greedy summarization is no longer valid.
If your model must support:
Joins over shared target nodes
Inter-path consistency
Global conditions
…then either you delay aggregation, or you accept that some post-processing must go
beyond the DAG style.
That’s a key boundary for the expressiveness of the model — but also a sharp indicator of
where it stays efficient and when it cannot.

Actually, I think that there may be a solution here.
Imagine our constraint DAG looks like this:
c1 <- c2.1 <- c3
c1 <- c2.2 <-c3
Where c2.1 and c2.2 are the two constraints on the
separate paths from c3 that are evaluated in parallel
during layer 2. Our problem was in requiring that these
two nodes know nothing about each other. But what if we
flattened them into a single c2?
Essentially:
score(v,c3) = max{ forall (v,u): match((v,u),c3) }
score(v,c2) = max{ forall (v,u): match((v,u),c2.1) *
match((v,u),c2.2) + score(u,c3) }
score(v,c1) = max{ forall (v,u): match((v,u),c1) + score(u,c2)
}
Though, this doesn't quite work, does it? in this setup, the
score is calculated for two edges to *the same node*,

rather than different ones. So we'd actually need to keep
scores for every *pair* of nodes that leads from another
node... kind of a supernode. Graph preprocessing might be
able to help here -- flattening diamond structures into a
single node-like value?

You're very close, and your thinking is heading in a fruitful direction — you're essentially
trying to preserve joint path information across constraint chains, which is the key to
solving the issue introduced by “diamond joins” (i.e., requiring that two subpaths
ultimately resolve to the same intermediate or terminal node).
Let me help refine the direction you're headed in and clarify why the naive flattening
doesn't work as-is, and what structure would be needed to support this.

🔁 What You're Trying to Preserve
You're trying to enforce a constraint like:
There exist two paths, each satisfying different constraints, that converge at the same
node y .
Your example DAG:
c1 <- c2.1 <- c3
<- c2.2 <- c3

has two chains going through c3 to c1 via different constraints — but c1 requires that
the outputs of both c2.1 and c2.2 map to the same node.

🧠 Why Flattening as score(v, c2) = f(c2.1, c2.2) Fails
Your idea to merge c2.1 and c2.2 into a single c2 works only if the outputs of both
paths share the same node, but your max{ match + score } formulation can't enforce
that.
Why?
Because each branch ( c2.1 , c2.2 ) could find its own best match, but the pair of matches
might not be consistent (i.e., not the same u ).

So you need something more expressive than score(v) :
You need to preserve information about sets or tuples of possible bindings.

✅ A Concrete Solution: Pairwise Binding Tables
You can generalize score(v, c) to score(v, u, c) — a 2D binding table that tells you:
For starting node v , how well does u satisfy constraint c as part of the path?
So to handle the constraint “both paths must end at the same y ”, you construct
something like:

score1[v, y] = match_path_1(v → y)
score2[v, y] = match_path_2(v → y)
joint_score[v, y] = score1[v, y] + score2[v, y]

# only if both defined

Python

Run

Then:

score[v] = max_y joint_score[v, y]
Python

Run

This solves your diamond join problem: instead of greedily selecting the best path from
each chain, you compute all candidate pairs of outputs and look for joint consistency at
y.

🧱 Practical Implementation
You can structure this using tensor-style operations, e.g., shape [|V| x |V|] matrices
holding partial path results.
This can be implemented via sparse tensor computations on the GPU — especially with
libraries like:
cuSPARSE
PyTorch sparse tensors
Deep Graph Library (DGL)

or GraphBLAS

Flattening to “supernodes” as you proposed may also be viable, but that tends to:
Precompute away ambiguity (good for static graphs)

But introduces combinatorial explosion when generalized
May struggle with dynamic graphs or changing ontologies
Instead, computing intermediate score tables per constraint chain and joining them just
before aggregation is often more flexible and general.

✨ Key Insight
This structure moves your model toward:
Tuple-based semantics, like in Datalog or SQL
Or path-constrained joins, like in SPARQL 1.1 property path joins
And it keeps the key benefit of your original method:
Efficient path traversal and scoring via GPU parallelism — but now with cross-chain join
capability.

So yes — your new proposal fixes the core limitation without giving up GPU compatibility

It’s no longer just a pattern matcher — it’s a hybrid constraint solver that makes localized

Can you remind me - we split up our system into
evaluating each constraint separately to avoid excess GPU
costs, right?

If we reduced that whole diamond to a single constraint
node -- that is, if we had a single GPU pass generate
score(v) by evaluating c1, c2, c3, and c4 all at once -- we
would end up with reduced efficiency due to needing to
make too many hops during a single pass, right? Or would
that honestly not be too much of a concern compared to
the amount of overhead having extra GPU passes per
constraint node costs?

Great question — and yes, we did originally split the system into one GPU pass per
constraint node to minimize per-pass complexity and memory usage, especially since
evaluating long or branching paths in a single kernel can quickly become inefficient. But
let's take a moment to break this down carefully.

🚀 GPU Pass per Constraint Node: Why We Do It
We split evaluation into multiple GPU passes, one per constraint node (or layer in the
DAG), because:
1. Memory locality is improved: Each pass focuses on only one constraint, using a
narrow slice of data (e.g., edges with a specific predicate).
2. Simpler computation: Each kernel is simple — e.g., apply match() and aggregate
max() , without needing to trace or manage long paths.

3. Avoid combinatorial explosion: For DAGs with joins or multiple options, doing
everything in one pass would mean tracking an exponential number of path
permutations, which is too expensive.
So yes, the split helps avoid branching logic and deep traversal inside a single GPU
kernel, which is a known source of inefficiency.

💡 But Would Merging the Diamond Help?
Let’s say you merged a diamond-shaped structure:
c1
/
c2.1
\

\
c2.2
/
c3

into a single “super constraint” node, so the GPU kernel computes the whole structure at
once.
You would gain:
Fewer GPU passes (1 instead of 3)
No need to hold intermediate score tables between passes
But you would lose:
Parallelism across constraint chains (each constraint is no longer independently
evaluated)
Branching avoidance: You’d now need to support conditional paths like “check both
c2.1 and c2.2, and ensure they lead to the same node”
GPU memory usage might spike: You need to hold all intermediate matches and join
logic within one pass — this means possibly building a [|V| x |V| x k] structure in
memory just to track candidate paths and their alignment.
And worst of all: hardcoding structure in the kernel makes generalization and reuse much
harder. If you want to change the query topology, you have to regenerate the kernel logic
or reinterpret the graph in a new way.

⚖️ Trade-off Summary
Metric

Per-Constraint Passes

Fused Super-Constraint

GPU kernel complexity

✅ Low

❌ High

Memory use

✅ Small + composable

❌ Potentially large

Code reuse &

✅ Easy (modular passes)

❌ Harder (tied logic)

❌ Higher (multiple

✅ One pass

generalization

Efficient for shallow paths

✅ Yes

✅ Yes

🧠 So When Would Fusing Be Better?

Fusing constraints into a single pass might be a win if:
The constraint DAG is shallow but dense, i.e. a small number of layers but with heavy
intra-layer dependencies
The number of hops required is small — e.g. two or three at most — so GPU kernels
don’t hit memory pressure or branching explosion
You have hard constraints that cannot be decomposed independently (e.g., a join that
absolutely requires seeing multiple edges at once)
You’re willing to do kernel specialization per query — not a general-purpose system,
but an optimized one for specific patterns

✅ Recommendation
Keep the per-constraint-node passes as your default, since:
It’s modular
It maps well to DAGs with branching structure
It enables fuzziness, weights, and score propagation to remain simple
But if you find a small subgraph (like your diamond) that can't be decomposed without
major loss in accuracy or efficiency, then you can fuse just that part into a one-off
specialized kernel or preprocessing step.
This hybrid approach is common in real GPU systems — optimize the common path, and
specialize for edge cases when necessary.

What if we preprocess the graph and flatten diamonds
ahead of time?
If normally we have a <- b,c <- d, we could map shared
nodes like b,c to a single "supernode" (b,c). This would
have to be done carefully, to preserve all relevant data, but
essentially the end result would be a new node
representing the *pair* b,c. Basically, we avoid needing an
entire |V| x |V| score table -- instead, the score table is
reduced to |V| + |VPair|^2. Assuming that the vast majority
of pairs of arbitrary nodes won't form a diamond, it would
probably only see a slight bump compared to the size of
|V|^2 -- specifically, if there are k paths on average

between any pair of nodes (v,u) \in V, it would be |V| +
|V|*k. So only an issue for highly connected graphs

That’s a really clever idea — and yes, pre-flattening diamond joins into supernodes could
mitigate a lot of the score propagation and combinatorial cost issues without blowing up
GPU memory or requiring high-complexity kernels.
Let’s unpack it carefully.

🧱 The Core Idea
When you have a constraint DAG with converging paths like this:
c1
/
c2.1
\

\
c2.2
/
c3

...you normally need to independently score both paths (via c2.1 and c2.2 ) and
somehow coordinate their results when propagating back to c1 . This coordination is hard
when scores are stored per-node — because you need to know if the same node satisfied
both branches.
But instead, if you:
Preprocess the DAG to identify such diamonds
Build a "supernode" to represent the joint satisfaction of multiple constraints (e.g.
pairs of (v1, v2) )
Store results like score((v1, v2), c2) — not per-node but per-node-pair
...then you’re converting a hard coordination problem into a bounded-space tensor
propagation problem.

🧮 Memory Complexity
Normally, naive approaches to handle overlapping joins in path scoring run into:
O(|V|²) memory for every path step, if you want to track all pairs
But your idea keeps this tractable because:
You only create supernodes for actually intersecting path pairs

The number of such pairs is not O(|V|²) — it’s closer to O(k·|V|) for average k
neighbors per node
This is much more manageable.

✅ Benefits
No runtime join check needed: the matching is already pre-resolved into a flat form
Avoids global combinatorics: doesn’t compute the full cartesian product of
possibilities
Preserves GPU parallelism: you still evaluate flat score tables per pass
Extensible: this could be generalized to N-way joins (triplets, etc.) if needed, though
the memory cost grows

⚠️ Caveats
Some things to watch out for:
1. Supernode explosion in worst-case topologies: highly connected graphs could still
generate O(|V|²) intermediate pairs. You may need heuristics to:
Prune unlikely matches
Discard weak joins early
Limit the join depth or fan-in width
2. Score traceability: once you start working with composite nodes like (b, c), your
backtracing logic gets more complex. You’ll need to retain metadata mapping these
to original nodes and paths for result interpretation.
3. Constraint preservation: If c2.1 and c2.2 are different constraints, you must
carefully retain enough per-constraint match information in your supernode
representation (e.g., was (b, c) a match because both matched strongly, or just
one?).

🧠 Alternative Analogy: Tensor Join Space
You're essentially constructing a compressed join tensor, where:
Each element is (v1, v2) only if both v1 and v2 exist in compatible paths for some
c_k

The tensor slice is sparse and only populated where true intersections occur
This is analogous to how systems like tensor contraction in GNNs

or sparse matrix

factorization work in graph computation — and they do run on GPUs quite effectively

when designed carefully.

🔧 Possible Implementation Plan
1. DAG pass: statically analyze your DAG to find diamonds
2. Prepass on KG: for each diamond structure, scan the KG and build a set of valid node
pairs (or n-tuples)
3. Flatten: store the supernodes and transform the DAG into a new one using them
4. Evaluate: reuse your per-constraint GPU propagation logic with these new
supernodes
5. Postprocess: backtrack results from supernodes to original node bindings

✨ Conclusion
Yes — this is a very promising direction. Pre-flattening joins into supernodes gives you:
Preservation of DAG-based scoring simplicity
Support for hard joins and value co-occurrence
Limited and bounded GPU memory impact in practice
And best of all — it gives you a viable way to fuzzily match joined structures without
sacrificing score consistency or efficiency.
You’re building your own kind of fuzzy SPARQL JOIN optimizer — but with GPU reasoning
baked in.

Let's move on from the diamond question and look at the
existing system again. In terms of evaluating multiple DAG
chains in parallel: how large do you think it could get
before overhead and GPU limitations would kick in? E.g. if I
have instance nodes V, with average degree D, and
concepts I want to match from an ontology C, where at
most any one concept takes at most K steps to evaluate it
(layers), what would the limitations of the system be?
In theory, I could evaluate each score(v \in V, c \in C_k) in
parallel -- the only requirement being it would need to
evaluate each layer C_k sequentially. However, I expect
that there would probably be limits to how much of the in-

theory parallelizable computation could *actually* be
parallelized. Given your understanding of the system we
talked about so far, what do you think?

Great question — and it strikes at the heart of the tradeoff between in-principle
parallelism and practical hardware constraints in GPU-based inference over constraint
DAGs. Let’s break it down with your parameters:

🔢 Definitions Recap
|V| — number of nodes (instances) in your KG
D — average out-degree (i.e. number of edges to consider during constraint
evaluation)
|C| — number of concepts in the ontology
K — maximum number of DAG layers required to evaluate any one concept
We’re scoring score(v, c) for all v ∈ V , c ∈ C

⚙️ Theoretical Workload
In the ideal case:
Each (v, c) pair can be evaluated independently
Each layer in a DAG must be evaluated sequentially, but layers themselves are uniform
GPU passes
Within a layer, you scan all neighbors of v (on edges of interest) and apply the
constraints
So for each layer:
Total number of constraint evaluations = |V| × |C_active| × D
Where C_active ⊆ C is the number of concepts that have constraints on this
layer
If K layers are needed to evaluate a concept, you’ll need K GPU passes per concept — or
ideally, group them by DAG-layer depth and re-use passes for multiple concepts.

🧠 Parallelism vs Practical Limits
GPUs love uniform, shallow, massive arrays of work — but suffer when:

Bottleneck

When It Hits

SM/thread oversubscription

If `

Warp divergence

If constraints vary wildly in complexity (e.g. some require multiple
neighbor types, others none), branches inside a kernel cause
slowdown

Memory pressure

Your score tensor score[v][c] must often stay in GPU memory
across layers (especially for fuzzy scoring), and large DAGs can
inflate this

Inter-layer sync

Layers must wait for prior layer to finish — this isn't a dealbreaker,
but adds latency especially if memory writes are uncoalesced or
sparse

Occupancy fragmentation

If you have only a few concepts, or only a few nodes, your GPU
cores may not be fully utilized

🧮 Practical Estimations
Let’s say:
|V| = 10⁶ (million instances)
|C| = 10³ (thousand concepts)
D = 10 (degree)
K = 5 (max DAG depth)

Then your max raw work per layer is:
10^6 × 10^3 × 10 = 10^10 constraint checks per layer

For K = 5 layers, that’s 5 × 10¹⁰ operations. Even with fast kernels, that becomes
noticeable.
Now, realistic GPUs have:
~10,000 CUDA cores
~100–150 SMs
Can execute 10⁷ to 10⁸ operations/sec per kernel if memory-efficient
So the practical limit you’d start to feel slowdown at is likely somewhere around:
~10⁶–10⁷ score entries updated per layer

Score tables taking up multiple GB of VRAM if fully dense (e.g. 10⁶ × 10³ floats = 4
GB)

✅ What Works Well
Few layers (K < 10): you stay in low latency regimes
Moderate |C| (up to ~10⁴): still can compute per-concept scores across instances in a
large table
Sparse edge scanning (low D): less neighbor traversal
Constraint reuse: many concepts using the same DAG steps (merge steps together!)

🔁 Possible Optimizations
Chunking: evaluate |C| in batches to reduce memory pressure (e.g., 100 concepts at
a time)
Dynamic pruning: filter inactive branches once scores drop to 0
Specialization kernels: compile GPU code per DAG shape to avoid generic branching
Structure-based grouping: compile shared DAGs for similar concepts (avoiding perconcept kernels)

💡 Summary: Rule of Thumb
Component

Scales well to:

Number of nodes

Up to 10⁷ on commodity GPUs

Number of concepts

10²–10⁴ before batch splitting

Average degree D

10–30 without major penalty

Layers (K)

Up to ~10 with good sync

Score memory

~1–4 GB = 10⁶ × 10³ float matrix

If you keep things in these regimes, the GPU can handle it efficiently and see real
advantages over CPU-style inferencing — especially in fuzzy reasoning or semantic
similarity domains where CPU-bound reasoners falter.
If you breach these limits, you’re either looking at batch-based execution, VRAM paging,
or redesigning the DAGs into more layered, hierarchical passes.

tool based on your actual ontology/KG stats.

My advisor has encouraged me not to use the word

Your advisor is giving you solid advice—“fuzzy” carries a very specific meaning in formal
logic, especially in fuzzy set theory and fuzzy description logics. If your method isn’t
formally grounded in that theory, it’s better to choose a more general or descriptive term
that reflects what you're doing (i.e., graded, similarity-based, approximate, or soft
matching), while avoiding confusion with established terminology.
Here are some alternatives, depending on what you want to emphasize:

🔹 Emphasizing Similarity or Approximate Matching
If your system computes degrees of match along paths, e.g., by measuring semantic
similarity between properties or classes:
Similarity-based typed path matching
Approximate path constraint satisfaction
Semantic path matching
Graded path matching
Soft path matching
These terms suggest that you're not enforcing crisp, Boolean path constraints, but
evaluating them by some scalar score, e.g., how closely a predicate matches a constraint.

🔹 Emphasizing Constraint Relaxation / Score Aggregation
If your method frames the query as a constraint satisfaction problem and then relaxes
those constraints into scores:
Relaxed constraint path matching
Score-aggregated path reasoning

Constraint-weighted path matching

🔹 Emphasizing Heuristics or Greedy Evaluation
If the key novelty is your greedy scoring algorithm or GPU acceleration:
Greedy path scoring algorithm
Max-score path matching
Parallel soft path evaluation

🔹 Emphasizing Intended Application Domain
If this is being used in service of ontology population, conceptual blending, or KG
completion:
Conceptual similarity path evaluation
Ontology-guided soft path scoring
Heuristic semantic path tracing

Suggested Primary Term
Given the context of your project (conceptual blending, GPU DAG evaluation, graded
match scoring), I’d suggest calling it:
"Similarity-based typed path matching"
That:
Evokes the semantic layer you’re reasoning over,
Makes clear it's about paths and types (e.g., predicates/classes in a KG),
Avoids the formal baggage of “fuzzy,”
Leaves you room to later say how similarity is computed (embedding distance, lexical
match, ontology alignment, etc.).
You could also footnote that this generalizes classic path constraint satisfaction by
assigning graded scores to approximate matches.

what about "partial typed path matching"?

“Partial typed path matching” is a solid candidate, and it brings a slightly different
connotation than “similarity-based.” It emphasizes the idea that:
Not all constraints need to be satisfied completely,
The method allows partial alignment of a typed path query against the KG,
Scoring or evaluation is permitted even when some edges or types don’t fully match.
Let’s weigh it:

✅ Pros of “Partial Typed Path Matching”
Accurate and descriptive: Captures that not all constraints are matched strictly.
Avoids formal fuzziness: No assumption of fuzzy logic theory, while still signaling
non-binary evaluation.
Plays well with path constraint terminology: Especially relevant for communities
familiar with SPARQL property paths, path queries in GDBs, and path-constrained
reasoning.
Evokes graph structure and query alignment: Useful if your thesis emphasizes
patterns over inferences.

⚠️ Possible Considerations
The word partial can imply incompleteness rather than graded scoring. This might raise
questions: "partial in what way?"
You might want to clarify early on that “partial” refers to degree of match rather
than length of path or presence/absence of data.
It's slightly less intuitive than “similarity-based” when talking about graded match
strength.

Suggested Usage
To make the intent absolutely clear, you could adopt one of the following naming variants:
Partial semantic path matching
Partial path constraint scoring
Graded typed path matching

Similarity-aware typed path matching
Path scoring with partial constraint satisfaction
Or just stick with:
Partial Typed Path Matching: A method for evaluating paths in a knowledge graph
against a set of typed property and object constraints, allowing for inexact or
incomplete matches and assigning scores to reflect degrees of alignment.
This phrasing keeps it precise while sidestepping formal “fuzzy” logic terminology.

Uniform computation per constraint layer
Array-like propagation of scores

Alright. Since it's been a while since we last discussed this,
can you help me by defining a specification of what exactly
this program will do?

Yeah, this is a good time to write down a clean spec. Let’s define what this system is, what
it takes in, what it computes, and what it definitely does not try to do.
I’ll describe it like you’d put in a thesis or design doc.

1. High-level description
The program is a GPU-accelerated reasoning engine for partial typed path matching
over knowledge graphs.
Given:
a knowledge graph (entities, properties, classes),
an ontology (class definitions expressed as structural constraints), and
optionally, similarity functions over types/properties,
it computes, for each node v and each target concept C :
a score measuring how well v matches the structural/ontological patterns defining C ,
and

optionally, an explanation in the form of a best-matching path (or small subgraph)
supporting that score.
The key characteristics:
Uses typed path constraints compiled into DAGs.
Runs constraint evaluation in batched, layered passes on the GPU.
Supports graded/partial matching, not just exact yes/no.
Targets large KGs with many nodes, and ontologies where concepts are defined by
patterns of bounded depth.

2. Data model
2.1 Knowledge graph G
Nodes V : individuals/entities.
Edges E ⊆ V × P × V : labeled by properties p ∈ P .
Class membership:
rdf:type edges: (v, rdf:type, C) say “v is an instance of class C ”.

Class hierarchy:
rdfs:subClassOf edges: (C1 , subClassOf, C2 ) say “C1 ⊑ C2 ”.
​

​

​

​

Internally, the graph is stored in GPU-friendly sparse adjacency format, e.g.:
CSR-like arrays per property:
neighbors_p[v] = contiguous block of neighbors via property p .

Typed node/class information:
types[v] = set or encoded vector of classes for v .

2.2 Ontology and constraints
The ontology is a set of class expressions (subset of OWL-like constructs). For each
concept C , we allow:
Atomic class: C .
Subclass chains: C ⊑ D .
Intersection: C ≡ A ⊓ B .
Union: C ≡ A ⊔ B .
Existential restrictions: C ⊑ ∃ R.D .
Universal restrictions (bounded): C ⊑ ∀ R.D (interpreted over known outgoing
edges).
Simple cardinality-like patterns: e.g. “at least N outgoing R to type D” (for now, local
counts).
Complement/disjointness in a limited, score-based way (e.g. penalizing overlap).

These class expressions are compiled into constraint DAGs.

3. Internal constraint DAG representation
For each concept C , the system compiles a constraint DAG:
Nodes in the DAG = constraint nodes.
Each constraint node encodes a local test, for example:
“current node has type in set T ”
“current node has an outgoing edge with property in P to a node that matches
child-constraint k ”
“intersection/union of child constraints”
Edges in the DAG express dependency between constraints:
From “deeper” constraints (on neighbors/types) up to “higher-level” concepts.
The DAG is layered so that:
Layer L can be evaluated using only scores from layers > L (for backward
propagation).
Intersection/union constraints become fan-in/fan-out patterns with appropriate
aggregation (min/max).
Multiple concepts may share DAG substructures; those are shared to avoid duplication.

4. Inputs / Outputs
4.1 Inputs
1. Knowledge graph G = (V , E, P )
2. Ontology O : OWL/RDFS-like axioms, restricted to the supported fragment.
3. Target concept set Ctarget ⊆ Classes:
​

The concepts we want to compute scores for.
4. Similarity / scoring configuration (optional):
Similarity functions:

sim_class(C1 , C2 ) ∈ [0, 1]
sim_prop(p1 , p2 ) ∈ [0, 1]
​

​

​

​

Aggregation settings: choice between exact (0/1) or graded, weights, thresholds.

4.2 Outputs
For each v ∈ V and each C ∈ Ctarget :
​

Score s(v, C) ∈ [0, 1]
1 = perfect structural match according to constraints

0 = no evidence of match
intermediate values = partial/approximate satisfaction
Optionally:
Top-k concepts per node: sorted list of C with highest scores for a given v .
Top-k nodes per concept: for each C , best-matching v .
Explanations: for each high-scoring (v, C), a path (or small tree) summarizing
the constraints that contributed most to the score.

5. Algorithmic behavior (pipeline)
5.1 Preprocessing
1. Load KG and ontology.
2. Build GPU-friendly graph indices:
CSR-like adjacency lists grouped by property.
Fast access to rdf:type and subClassOf information.
3. Compile ontology → constraint DAGs:
For each concept C ∈ Ctarget , parse its class expression(s).
​

Recursively construct constraint nodes:
Atomic classes
Intersections/unions
Restriction nodes ( ∃ R.D , ∀ R.D , simple cardinality)
Link them into a DAG with clear layer structure (from leaves up).
Optionally:
Identify shared subgraphs across concepts.
Compress / reuse shared constraint nodes.

5.2 Evaluation on GPU
The core computation is layered score propagation.
We maintain:
For each constraint node k and each graph node v , a score sk (v).
​

Evaluation runs backwards from leaves to roots:
1. Initialize leaf scores:
For nodes that directly test atomic type membership or simple local properties,
initialize sk (v) based on:
​

rdf:type assertions (exact match)

subclass closure (precomputed, if used)
similarity functions (for graded matching)

2. For each layer ℓ from deepest to shallowest:
Launch a GPU kernel that updates all sk (v) for constraint nodes in layer ℓ, for all
​

v ∈V.
Per constraint node type:
Type constraint:
Uses existing type information or scores from child constraints.
Existential restriction ( ∃ R.D ):
For each v , look at neighbors u reachable via properties in R.
Aggregate over neighbors:

sk (v) = maxu∈NR (v) [sim_prop(edgeProp, R) ⋅ schild (u)].
​

​

​

​

Universal restriction ( ∀ R.D ):
For each v , combine scores over all neighbors via R (e.g. using min or a
penalty for bad neighbors).
Intersection:
For each v , combine children: sk (v) = minj schildj (v).
​

​

​

​

Union:
For each v , combine children: sk (v) = maxj schildj (v).
​

​

​

​

Simple cardinality:
Count neighbors satisfying child constraints; transform count into a
score.
Each such update is a SIMD-friendly kernel: all nodes and constraint nodes in the
layer are updated in parallel, using CSR adjacency.
3. Final aggregation:
For each concept C , identify its root constraint node kC .
​

Define the concept score:

s(v, C) := skC (v)
​

​

4. Optional postprocessing:
Normalize or rescale scores.
Extract top-k matches / explanations by tracing back the highest-contributing
neighbors and child constraints.

6. Modes and semantics
6.1 Exact (logical-style) mode
All matches are binary:
type matches → 0/1
property matches → 0/1
Aggregation is via min / max on those booleans.

This approximates a tractable fragment of OWL/RDFS-style reasoning via path-based
constraint satisfaction, with GPU acceleration.

6.2 Graded / similarity-based mode
Type and property matches use similarity functions:
lexical, embedding-based, or ontology-based similarity
Aggregation uses:
max for “exists some satisfying path”
min or other t-norm-like functions for “all constraints must jointly hold”

Scores are degrees of alignment with the intended concept pattern, used for:
conceptual blending,
noisy LLM-extracted KGs,
approximate type inference, etc.

6.3 Optional hybrid OWA/CWA bit semantics
You previously sketched a [truth, openness] bit-pair per (v, C) :
[1,1] = definitively in C
[0,1] = definitively not in C
[1,0] = tentatively in C (closed-world default)
[0,0] = unknown / no evidence

This can be layered on top of the score semantics, but I’d treat it as an extension to the
core spec:
A separate mode that annotates scores with epistemic state (what is definitely known
vs default-assumed).

7. Limitations / non-goals
The program does not aim to:
Implement full OWL DL reasoning.
Handle:
arbitrary nominals,
identity reasoning ( owl:sameAs ),
arbitrary cardinality constraints,
unbounded role chains,
general cyclic TBox reasoning.
Replace SPARQL engines for full query workloads:
It’s not optimized for arbitrary joins, filters, ORDER BY, etc.
Guarantee logically complete entailment in the DL sense.

Instead, it is explicitly:
A constraint-based, path-structured scoring engine,
That can approximate a useful subset of logical inference,
While supporting graded match strength and GPU scale.

If you’d like, next step could be:
Turn this into a 1–2 page “System Model” section for your thesis, or
Specialize it into an API-style spec (e.g. functions like compile_constraints ,
evaluate_concepts , explain(v,C) ), or

Narrow it down to the minimal subset you want to implement for the first prototype.

# For now, one CSR per property

2. Phase 2: Constraint Nodes + DAG Representation
Goal: Represent a single concept as a small DAG of constraint nodes that will be evaluated
over the graph.

Constraint node types
In constraints.py :

from dataclasses import dataclass
from enum import Enum, auto
class ConstraintType(Enum):
ATOMIC_CLASS = auto()
# node has (possibly similar) type C
EXISTS_RESTRICTION = auto() # ∃ R.D
FORALL_RESTRICTION = auto() # ∀ R.D (later)
INTERSECTION = auto()
UNION = auto()
# Add more later (cardinality, etc.)
@dataclass
class ConstraintNode:
idx: int
ctype: ConstraintType
# For ATOMIC_CLASS
class_idx: int | None = None
# For EXISTS_RESTRICTION / FORALL_RESTRICTION
prop_idx: int | None = None
# index into graph.offsets_p
child_indices: list[int] | None = None # indices into DAG nodes





Example: concept “ThingWithWheel”
Let’s say:
Concept C: “nodes that have a hasPart neighbor of type Wheel”
In OWL-ish: C ⊑ ∃ hasPart.Wheel
In examples.py :

from .constraints import ConstraintNode, ConstraintDAG, ConstraintType
def make_toy_constraint_dag():
# Node 0: ATOMIC_CLASS Wheel
atomic_wheel = ConstraintNode(
idx=0,
ctype=ConstraintType.ATOMIC_CLASS,
class_idx=1 # Wheel
)
# Node 1: EXISTS hasPart.Wheel
exists_hasPart_wheel = ConstraintNode(
idx=1,
ctype=ConstraintType.EXISTS_RESTRICTION,
prop_idx=0,
# hasPart
child_indices=[0],
# child is atomic_wheel
)
nodes = [atomic_wheel, exists_hasPart_wheel]
# layer 1: leaf (atomic), layer 0: exists node
layers = [[1], [0]]
return ConstraintDAG(nodes=nodes, root_idx=1, layers=layers)

That’s the smallest nontrivial DAG: a leaf constraint and one existential above it.

3. Phase 3: GPU Evaluation for One DAG, One Concept
Goal: Implement the layered propagation engine that takes a KGraph and a
ConstraintDAG and returns s(v,C) for all nodes.

In dag_eval.py :

import torch
from .graph import KGraph
from .constraints import ConstraintDAG, ConstraintType



def eval_dag_scores(graph: KGraph, dag: ConstraintDAG, device='cuda'):
# Move graph to device if not already
offsets_p = [off.to(device) for off in graph.offsets_p]
neighbors_p = [neigh.to(device) for neigh in graph.neighbors_p]
node_types = graph.node_types.to(device)
num_nodes = graph.num_nodes



# Allocate score tensor: [num_nodes, num_constraint_nodes]
num_constraints = len(dag.nodes)
scores = torch.zeros((num_nodes, num_constraints), device=device)
# You can process layer-by-layer; here we'll just loop in Python first
for layer in reversed(dag.layers):
for node_idx in layer:
cnode = dag.nodes[node_idx]
if cnode.ctype == ConstraintType.ATOMIC_CLASS:
# Exact: 1 if node_types[v, class_idx] == 1

This inner loop is intentionally simple/naive for now; you’ll later:
Replace the Python for v in range(num_nodes) loop with a vectorized or custom
CUDA kernel (e.g. segment-reduce pattern using offsets ).
Add similarity-based scoring (e.g. sim_class matrices).
But this already gives you an MVP: one concept, one DAG, evaluated across all nodes on
GPU (or CPU if you switch device).

4. Phase 4: Multiple Concepts, Shared Constraints
Once the single-concept case works and you can see sensible outputs, extend to multiple
concepts in parallel:
Either:
Evaluate each ConstraintDAG separately and stack the resulting s(v,C) ,
Or merge DAGs that share subtrees into a super-DAG and keep a single scores
tensor with multiple roots.
Minimal approach for now: loop over concepts.

def eval_multiple_concepts(graph, dags, device='cuda'):
all_scores = []
for dag in dags:
s = eval_dag_scores(graph, dag, device=device)
all_scores.append(s.unsqueeze(1)) # [num_nodes, 1]
return torch.cat(all_scores, dim=1) # [num_nodes, num_concepts]

Later you can deduplicate DAG nodes and compute scores for all constraints at once.

5. Phase 5: Ontology → DAG Compiler (Minimal Fragment)
Once the core engine works on hand-constructed DAGs, build a baby compiler that
translates a restricted OWL fragment into these ConstraintDAG structures.
In ontology_parse.py , starting small:
1. Use rdflib to load a TTL file.
2. For a single named class ex:TargetClass , parse:
ex:TargetClass rdfs:subClassOf ex:SomeClass
ex:TargetClass rdfs:subClassOf [ a owl:Restriction; owl:onProperty ex:p;
owl:someValuesFrom ex:D ]
ex:TargetClass owl:intersectionOf ( ex:A ex:B )

3. Produce ConstraintNode s accordingly:
ATOMIC_CLASS for ex:A , ex:B , ex:D
EXISTS_RESTRICTION nodes for someValuesFrom
INTERSECTION nodes for intersectionOf

You don’t need full parsing at first; you can start with one or two pattern types hardcoded.

6. Phase 6: Performance + Features
Once the MVP works:

Optimize neighbor aggregation
Replace the naive per-node loop in existential restrictions with something like:
Use a segment max reduction:
child_scores[neighbors] → segmented by offsets → max per segment.

You can:
Use PyTorch’s scatter_reduce if structured suitably,
Or write a small CUDA kernel later.

Add graded/partial matching
Introduce sim_class and sim_prop matrices:

sim_class = torch.eye(num_classes, device=device)
sim_prop = torch.eye(num_props, device=device)

elif cnode.ctype == ConstraintType.INTERSECTION:
# all children share same node v
child_scores = torch.stack([scores[:, ci] for ci in cnode.child_indices], dim
scores[:, node_idx] = child_scores.min(dim=1).values
elif cnode.ctype == ConstraintType.UNION:
child_scores = torch.stack([scores[:, ci] for ci in cnode.child_indices], dim
scores[:, node_idx] = child_scores.max(dim=1).values

Yep, that output actually makes perfect sense given the current CSR… it’s our CSR that’s
lying to us 😅
Let’s debug it.

1. What the current CSR actually encodes
Right now in make_toy_graph() we have:

neighbors_p0 = torch.tensor([1, 2, 2], dtype=torch.int32)
offsets_p0
= torch.tensor([0, 2, 2, 3, 3], dtype=torch.int32)
Python

Offsets mean:
v = 0 : neighbors are neighbors[0:2] → [1, 2]
v = 1 : neighbors are neighbors[2:2] → []
v = 2 : neighbors are neighbors[2:3] → [2]
v = 3 : neighbors are neighbors[3:3] → []

So the actual edges in the graph are:
0 -> 1
0 -> 2
2 -> 2 (self-loop)

Not 3 -> 2 like we wrote in the comments earlier.
Given that, the results you got are exactly what they should be:
C1 ≡ ∃ hasPart.Wheel

Wheels are nodes 1 and 2
Node 0 has neighbors [1,2] → yes → score 1
Node 2 has neighbor [2] (Wheel) → yes → score 1
Nodes 1 and 3 have no outgoing edges → score 0
So: C1 = [1, 0, 1, 0] ✅
C2 ≡ FurnitureLike ⊓ C1

Furniture-like are nodes 0 and 3
Node 0: Furniture-like and C1=1 → min(1,1)=1
Node 3: Furniture-like and C1=0 → min(1,0)=0
Others: 0
So: C2 = [1, 0, 0, 0] ✅

Run

So the engine is correct, the graph encoding is just not the one we thought.

2. How to fix it to match the intended graph
If you really want:
0 -> 1
0 -> 2
3 -> 2

Then the CSR should be:
degrees:
deg(0) = 2
deg(1) = 0
deg(2) = 0
deg(3) = 1
cumulative offsets:
offsets[0] = 0
offsets[1] = 2 (0 had 2 neighbors)
offsets[2] = 2 (1 had 0)
offsets[3] = 2 (2 had 0)
offsets[4] = 3 (3 had 1 → total 3)

So change this line in make_toy_graph() :

# WRONG for 3 -> 2:
offsets_p0 = torch.tensor([0, 2, 2, 3, 3], dtype=torch.int32)
Python

Run

# CORRECT for edges: 0->1, 0->2, 3->2
offsets_p0 = torch.tensor([0, 2, 2, 2, 3], dtype=torch.int32)
Python

Run

to:

And keep:

neighbors_p0 = torch.tensor([1, 2, 2], dtype=torch.int32)
Python

Now the neighbors become:

Run

v0: [1,2]
v1: []
v2: []
v3: [2]

python -m src.examples
Bash

So literally the first attempt to move a tensor to "cuda" is failing. That means:
PyTorch sees CUDA ( torch.cuda.is_available() == True ),
But when it actually tries to create/use a context, the driver/runtime can’t give it a
usable device.
Your code is fine; this is system-level. Here’s how I’d debug it.

1. Confirm it’s not our code by using a tiny test
Open a Python REPL in the same environment you’re using for the project and run:

import torch
print("is_available:", torch.cuda.is_available())
print("device_count:", torch.cuda.device_count())
x = torch.tensor([1.0])
x_cuda = x.to("cuda") # <-- does this line fail?
print(x_cuda)

If that also throws CUDA-capable device(s) is/are busy or unavailable , it’s 100%
an environment/driver issue, not our DAG code.
If that works, then something very strange is going on (but I strongly expect it will fail
the same way).

2. Common causes of “busy or unavailable”
On a machine like yours (1650 Max-Q), typical culprits:
1. Driver / CUDA runtime mismatch
PyTorch was built with CUDA 12.1:
torch.version.cuda → '12.1'

But your installed NVIDIA driver might be too old for CUDA 12.1.
In that case, torch.cuda.is_available() can still return True , but as soon as it
tries to create a context, it fails.
2. Another process is holding the GPU in exclusive mode
Some tools (certain profilers, games, or another Python process) can grab the
GPU in a way that prevents new contexts.
On Linux, you’d check nvidia-smi . On Windows, you can:
Open NVIDIA-SMI in a terminal (if installed)
Or use Task Manager → Performance → GPU → see if any other app is
hammering it.
3. WSL / virtual environment weirdness
If you’re in WSL2 or some container-like environment, CUDA visibility can be
tricky: the host sees the GPU, but the guest can’t fully use it.
But since torch.cuda.is_available() is True, it suggests PyTorch thinks it’s okay,
but the runtime disagrees.
4. Stale/broken CUDA context
Occasionally, after a crash, the driver/runtime ends up in a weird state.
A full reboot often clears this.

3. Things to try concretely
A. Reboot & rerun minimal test
Not glamorous, but often effective:
1. Reboot the machine.
2. Run the tiny test again:

import torch
x = torch.tensor([1.0])
print(x.to("cuda"))
Python

If that suddenly works, your project will too.

Run

B. Check driver version vs CUDA
On Windows:
Right-click desktop → NVIDIA Control Panel → Help → System Information → look at
the driver version.
Compare it against the minimum driver version for CUDA 12.1 (you can look this up,
but ballpark: you want a fairly recent driver, not something from years ago).
If your driver is old, updating it via GeForce Experience or NVIDIA’s site is often the fix.

C. Add a graceful fallback in your code (for now)
While you debug, you can make your code not crash when CUDA is unusable:
In examples.py :

import torch
def get_device():
if torch.cuda.is_available():
try:
# Try a tiny allocation/move to confirm it's actually usable
_ = torch.tensor([0.0]).to("cuda")
return "cuda"
except RuntimeError:
print("Warning: CUDA appears available but is not usable, falling back to CPU")
return "cpu"
else:
return "cpu"

Replace it with:

if cnode.ctype == ConstraintType.ATOMIC_CLASS:
# Graded class matching using sim_class.
# node_types: [num_nodes, num_classes] (one-hot or multi-hot)
# sim_class: [num_classes, num_classes], where sim_class[i, j]
#
= similarity between stored class i and constraint class j.
# For constraint class_idx = k, we compute:
#
scores[:, k] = node_types @ sim_class[:, k]
class_idx = cnode.class_idx
# Column vector [num_classes] for this target class
col = sim_class[:, class_idx] # [num_classes]
# For each node v, score = sum_i node_types[v, i] * sim_class[i, class_idx]
scores[:, node_idx] = node_types @ col




The rest of eval_dag_scores (EXISTS, INTERSECTION, UNION, etc.) stays the same.
Now:
If you don’t pass sim_class , you get exact behavior.
If you pass a similarity matrix, nodes can match a class partially.

Step 2: Create a class similarity matrix in examples.py
We’ll build a simple similarity matrix:
class 0: Thing (unused)
class 1: Wheel
class 2: Furniture-like
We’ll say:
Wheel is exact for Wheel: sim(Wheel, Wheel) = 1
Furniture-like is somewhat similar to Wheel: sim(Furniture-like, Wheel) = 0.3
(and symmetric, if you want)

2.1 Add a helper in examples.py
Near the top of examples.py , add:

def make_class_similarity_matrix(num_classes: int) -> torch.Tensor:
"""
Construct a toy class similarity matrix sim_class[i, j] in [0,1],
where i is the stored class and j is the constraint class.

Here:
- class 1 = Wheel
- class 2 = Furniture-like
We will say Furniture-like has some similarity to Wheel.
"""
sim = torch.eye(num_classes, dtype=torch.float32)
# Make Furniture-like (2) partially similar to Wheel (1)
# sim_class[stored, constraint]
sim[2, 1] = 0.3 # a Furniture-like node partially matches 'Wheel' constraint
return sim

(You can also set sim[1, 2] = 0.3 if you want symmetric similarity, but only the column
“constraint class” matters for ATOMIC_CLASS .)

1. Exact mode (no sim_class )

Step 4: What you should expect
In your current toy graph:
node 1: Wheel
node 2: Wheel
node 0,3: Furniture-like
edges: 0 → [1,2], 3 → [2]
For constraint Wheel ( class_idx = 1 ):
Exact mode:
score(node1, Wheel) = 1
score(node2, Wheel) = 1
score(node0, Wheel) = 0
score(node3, Wheel) = 0
Similarity mode with sim[2, 1] = 0.3 :
score(node1, Wheel) = 1 (Wheel)
score(node2, Wheel) = 1 (Wheel)
score(node0, Wheel) = 0.3 (Furniture-like)
score(node3, Wheel) = 0.3 (Furniture-like)
For C1: ∃ hasPart.Wheel , in similarity mode:
Node 0 has neighbors {1,2} = both Wheels → max(1,1) = 1.0 (still exact, no partial
needed here).
Node 3 has neighbor {2} = Wheel → 1.0.
Nodes 1,2 have no outgoing edges → 0.0.
For C2: FurnitureLike ⊓ ∃ hasPart.Wheel , in similarity mode:
Node 0:
Furniture-like score for “Furniture-like” = 1
C1 score = 1
Intersection = min(1,1) = 1
Node 3:
Furniture-like score = 1
C1 score = 1

Node 1,2: 0

Yep, that output is totally consistent with what we coded and with this specific toy graph –
the similarity matrix just isn’t “visible” yet in the results.
Here’s why.

Why C1 looks identical (exact vs similarity)
Recall:
Classes:
1 = Wheel
2 = Furniture-like
Types:
node 0: Furniture-like
node 1: Wheel
node 2: Wheel
node 3: Furniture-like

Similarity matrix (what we set up):

sim_class = I
sim_class[2, 1] = 0.3

# stored=Furniture-like, constraint=Wheel

Concept C1:
C1 ≡ ∃ hasPart.Wheel

Edges:
0 -> [1, 2]
3 -> [2]
1,2 -> []

Evaluation:
1. Leaf constraint “Wheel” (ATOMIC_CLASS, class_idx = 1)
For nodes 1 and 2 (Wheel), score = 1
For nodes 0 and 3 (Furniture-like), score = 0.3 (via sim[2,1])
2. Existential “∃ hasPart.Wheel”
Node 0 has neighbors 1 and 2 → child scores {1, 1} → max = 1
Node 3 has neighbor 2 → child score 1 → max = 1
Nodes 1,2 have no neighbors → 0
So even though similarity would give 0.3 for Furniture-like-as-Wheel, it’s never needed:
every hasPart edge lands on a true Wheel, so the max is 1 either way. That’s why C1 is
identical in exact and similarity mode.

Why C2 also looks the same
C2:
C2 ≡ FurnitureLike ⊓ ∃ hasPart.Wheel

“FurnitureLike” is class 2.
In our sim matrix, we only changed sim[2,1]. We didn’t change anything for column 2
(constraint = Furniture-like), so ATOMIC_CLASS(Furniture-like) is still strictly 0/1.
Intersection is min( Furniture-like score, C1 score ) → same as before.
Result: C2 also unchanged.

If you want to see similarity do something
You need a case where:
A node doesn’t have an exact Wheel neighbor, but
It does have a neighbor with a class that is similar to Wheel.

For example, change the toy graph so:
No node is directly typed as Wheel.
Some nodes are only Furniture-like.
But you still use the constraint “Wheel”, and rely on sim(Furniture-like, Wheel) > 0.
Concretely, in make_toy_graph() :

# Instead of nodes 1,2 being Wheel:
# node_types[1, 1] = 1.0
# node_types[2, 1] = 1.0
# Make them Furniture-like instead:
node_types[1, 2] = 1.0
node_types[2, 2] = 1.0

Now:
No node has exact class “Wheel”.
Every hasPart neighbor is Furniture-like.
With sim[2,1] = 0.3, ATOMIC_CLASS(Wheel) gives 0.3 for those neighbors.
Then ∃ hasPart.Wheel will give 0.3 instead of 0 or 1.
You should see something like:
C1 exact:
C1 similar:

all 0
node 0 and 3 ≈ 0.3

That’s the behavior your similarity machinery is designed for; the current toy graph just
happens to always have perfect matches, so the graded part never “wins” over 1.0.
So short answer: your output is correct; we just need a slightly different toy setup to make
the similarity effect visible.

Let's move back to exact mode for a moment. Can we
come up with an example that will produce scores in
between 0 and 1, instead of everything being either 0 or
1?
What I mean by this: imagine the following example:
Something is a Vehicle if it hasPart some Wheel and

hasPart some Seat.
Consider the two instances below:
:Bicycle hasPart [a :Wheel; a :Seat] .
:Stool hasPart [a :Seat]
Since :Bicycle has both a Wheel and a Seat, it has 2/2
components of the intersection restriction met, and is
therefore scored as 1.0 Vehicle.
:Stool has only a Seat -- it therefore meets 1/2
components of the intersection, and we will (in this
circumstance) score it as 0.5 Vehicle.
In this example:
- Does this align with what we had discussed earlier about
similarity scoring, or is this new? I recall previously that we
had discussed using just min() for intersection, but maybe
we discussed partial matches as well?
- Can we extend our code to allow for some intersections
to have this "aggregate" scoring instead of pure min?

Nice example, that’s exactly the kind of “graded but still logical-ish” behavior this
framework is good at.
Let me split this into two parts: (1) conceptually what you’re doing, and how it relates to
what we discussed before, and (2) concrete code changes to support this kind of
“fractional intersection” in your current implementation.

1. Conceptual: is this “new” or consistent?
Your example:
Vehicle ≡ (hasPart some Wheel) ⊓ (hasPart some Seat)
Bicycle satisfies both ⇒ score 2/2 = 1.0 Vehicle
Stool satisfies only Seat ⇒ score 1/2 = 0.5 Vehicle
This is a different aggregation choice at the intersection level:

Before: we talked about using min for intersection:
intersection score = min(child scores)
That corresponds to a strict “all constraints must be satisfied to this degree”
semantics.
Now: you’re suggesting something like:
intersection score = (sum of satisfied constraints) / (number of constraints)
More generally: average (or weighted average) of child scores.
So:
It’s consistent with the graded/score-based framework we discussed.
It is a different scoring policy at the intersection node (not incompatible, just a
different choice).
You can reasonably support both:
strict intersection: min
“fractional coverage” intersection: mean (or weighted mean)
So yes, we can extend the code to allow some intersections to use this “aggregate”
scoring instead of pure min.

2. Extending the code: per-intersection aggregation mode
We’ll add:
A small enum (or just a string flag) to ConstraintNode to say how this intersection
should aggregate children.
Logic in eval_dag_scores to switch between min (old behavior) and mean (your 1/2
example).

That worked!
Just as a sanity check -- how close is what we created to

the original idea I proposed? I proposed the following:
---Assume that, for any node, we only care about the
similarity score for the *highest-scoring* match. Right? For
any given node, we want the score for the *best* path of
length m from that node... recursively, that's roughly:
def score(v_i, k):
if k=m: return 0
else return max(forall (v_i,_,v_j) in E: score(v_j, k+1))
So, if we start backwards and work our way to the
beginning... then that should be doable on a GPU, *if* we
are smart about it. It could look roughly like this:
- check every node for its match against c_{m-1}: base
case. each node s returns its highest-scoring (s,p,o) and
the score for it -- essentially, all nodes together have
created a score array of length |V|.
- in the next step (frontier?), feed that score array back in
to every node, and check against c_{m-2} for each (s,p,o).
when checking the score for (s, p, o), check c_{m-2} against
p,o and add the precomputed score for c_{m-1} for o -based on its highest-scoring neighbors, from the array -and again return the highest score to be fed into each
node in the next step. (s,p,o) can also be saved somewhere
for path reconstruction but it doesn't have to be fed in
- repeat until you reach the first node. then, you have each
node's score, and you have the paths for each node (could
be saved in a |V| x m bitmap).
...
is this method generalizable to structures
beyond linear paths?I think that would work to some
extent -- but I'm not quite sure. Let's see:
Imagine that the constraint subgraph is modeled as layers:
c_{k,l} is the lth constraint in layer k. So for some starting
node S, it might be matching against a subgraph like this:
S --P_{0,0}->O_{0,0}--P{1,0}->O_{1,0}
\
\-P_{0,1}->O_{0,1}--P{1,1}->O_{1,1}
\
\--P{1,2}->O_{1,2}
So the topology is a bit different, but it's still

decomposable into paths -- it's not really that different
from doing a linear constraint path of length 5, it's just
split apart more.
...
Full OWL DL isn't something I want to try to tackle. But
some subsets of DL can essentially be represented using
clever use of SPARQL property paths.
A very simple example: if A typeof C1, and C1 subClassOf*
C2, then A typeof C2.
Some more complex examples: intersections and unions.
Intersections and unions could basically be perfomed
using that exact same DAG-style approach.
How far could we take this? To what extent could
reasoning/inferencing be represented with DAG-style
backwards reasoning chains computed on the GPU?
Oh and -- if you're wondering about the Kleene star there,
I think it's less of a problem than it might appear at first.
My original conception involved DAGs of a specified
length -- but since all that needs to get propagated is the
score array, it could probably be extended to support
arbitrary-length paths.

Short version: we’re very close in spirit to what you originally sketched, but we’ve
implemented a “clean, smaller slice” of it so far. The core recurrence and DAG/backwardspropagation idea is in the code; what’s missing are (1) explicit multi-hop path chains as
first-class objects, (2) general property-path / Kleene-star style patterns, and (3) the more
global “ontology-level” reasoning you were imagining.
Let me map your original idea to what we have now.

1. Your original recurrence vs what the code does
You wrote (for a length-m constraint chain):
For any node, we want the score for the best path of length m…
score(v_i, k):
if k = m: return 0
else: max over (v_i, _, v_j) in E of score(v_j, k+1)

And then:

Evaluate the “last” constraint c_{m-1} for every node (base case),
Propagate backwards layer by layer,
Each node keeps the best score reachable via its neighbors,
At the end, each node has a score and you can reconstruct a best path.
What we have now:
Constraint DAG layers = your “k from m−1 back to 0”.
ATOMIC_CLASS leaf nodes = your base cases ( c_{m-1} , or more generally leaves of
the constraint graph).
EXISTS_RESTRICTION does:

score(v, exists R.D) = max_{(v, R, u)} score(u, D)
Python

Run

which is exactly the “max over neighbors” step you described, just without the extra
“+ local match” term yet.
We evaluate bottom-up over layers: leaves first, then their parents, etc., which is
equivalent to your backwards DP from depth m–1 to 0.
So the mathematical shape—“scores are computed as local tests on neighbors plus max
pooling over neighbors, applied layer by layer”—is the same as your original idea. We’ve
implemented it at the “one hop = one DP step” level.
Where we’ve not gone fully yet:
Your text assumes a linear chain of length m of constraints (c₀…cₘ₋₁). We’ve instead
generalized to:
A DAG of constraints (branching via INTERSECTION/UNION),
But we have only used short, shallow DAGs in the toy examples.
Your recurrence implicitly allows accumulating score along the path (e.g., add 1 or
some similarity at each step). Our EXISTS_RESTRICTION currently just pulls the child’s
score with a max; we haven’t added “+ local contribution for matching this step”
inside that neighbor loop yet.
So: the engine is implementing the backwards DP skeleton you described, but in a
simplified, compositional way.

2. Linear chains vs DAG-style patterns
You later generalized to:
c_{k,l} is the l-th constraint in layer k …
subgraphs like:

S --P_{0,0}->O_{0,0}--P{1,0}->O_{1,0}
\
\-P_{0,1}->O_{0,1}--P{1,1}->O_{1,1}
\
\--P{1,2}->O_{1,2}

Our current ConstraintDAG is exactly this idea:
Nodes are c_{k,l} (constraints at “layer” k, index l).
An EXISTS hasPart.Wheel node depends on its child ATOMIC_CLASS Wheel node.
An INTERSECTION(...) node depends on multiple children, i.e. branching structure
above a given layer.
So at an abstract level, you now have the DAG pattern representation you were aiming for.
Your Vehicle example:
Vehicle ≡ ∃hasPart.Wheel ⊓ ∃hasPart.Seat
is literally implemented as:
Leaf class constraints for Wheel and Seat ,
Two ∃ nodes for each property restriction,
One INTERSECTION node combining them.
That is your DAG pattern matcher; we’ve just not yet injected long chains (P₀,O₀,P₁,O₁, …)
as multi-step property paths.
If we wanted to get closer to the exact initial “path of length m” formulation, we’d:
Represent each step c_k as an EXISTS node with both a property type test and an
object type test.
Chain them linearly: c_{m-1} leaf → c_{m-2} → … → c_0 .
For fuzzy or incremental scoring, add a local contribution at each step inside the
EXISTS loop.

But conceptually, we’d still be using the same ConstraintDAG + layered backward
evaluation.

3. Intersections, unions, and fractional coverage
You asked:
Intersections and unions could basically be performed using that exact same DAG-style
approach.

To what extent can reasoning be represented with DAG-style backwards reasoning
chains on the GPU?
We’ve now:
Implemented INTERSECTION with:
MIN aggregation = strict logical “and” (all children must be high).
MEAN aggregation = fractional coverage (“you satisfy k out of n components” →

your Vehicle/Seat/Wheel example).
Implemented UNION as max across children.
This is exactly what you were gesturing at: build more complex class descriptions by
combining simpler constraint nodes in a DAG, and evaluate them by backward DP over
layers. The fractional intersection version is a small, but natural, extension you came up
with recently—consistent with the original score-based view, but not explicitly in the very
first sketch.

4. SPARQL property paths, Kleene star, and OWL-ish reasoning
This is where we’re still some distance away from your longer-term vision:
You mentioned:
some subsets of DL can essentially be represented using clever use of SPARQL property
paths
… e.g., A rdf:type C1 and C1 rdfs:subClassOf* C2 ⇒ A rdf:type C2
… intersections and unions via the same DAG-style approach
… and possibly Kleene star for arbitrary-length paths.
Right now, we have:
Finite-depth DAGs only: all constraints are in an acyclic graph with explicit layers; no
arbitrary-length loops.
A single-step EXISTS that looks at immediate neighbors in one property bucket.
No explicit encoding of:
subClassOf* closure,
type vs subClassOf vs other properties as special,

Kleene-star / recursive “keep propagating until fixpoint” behavior.
We could get closer to that original goal by:
Treating subClassOf as a special property:
Build a DAG that iteratively propagates class membership up the hierarchy,

Or implement an explicit fixpoint loop: repeatedly feed the scores back until
stable.
Allowing certain DAGs to be re-applied as long as scores change (your “arbitrarylength but only propagate score arrays” idea).
Adding a notion of “concept-defined-by-path” where a DAG node effectively
represents a SPARQL property path pattern.
But we haven’t done any of that yet; right now, we’re handling finite, explicitly-bounded
patterns.

5. How close are we, in one sentence?
The code you’ve written is a concrete, working instance of the core idea you
proposed:
Backwards, layer-wise scoring over a constraint DAG,
With max pooling over neighbors (your max over (v_i,_,v_j) recurrence),
And compositional combination of constraints (intersections/unions).
What’s missing compared to the full original vision:
Explicit multi-hop typed path chains as first-class constraints,
Path-wise score accumulation (beyond our simple Vehicle example),
Recursive / fixpoint behavior for property paths (Kleene star, subClassOf*),
The more ambitious OWL-ish reasoning layer on top.
If you want to steer closer to the original proposal next, the natural step would be:
Pick one multi-hop typed path pattern (e.g. length-2 or length-3 chain),
Implement it as a linear constraint chain in the DAG,
Add a local match contribution per step (property type + object type),
And verify that you can see “best path” scores behave the way you initially imagined.
That would directly bridge from “we have the skeleton” to “we’re now explicitly doing the
m-length typed path matching that kicked this whole idea off.”

let's go for it -- can you help me with adding that next
step?

Yeah, we’re ready to make this “real” multi-hop thing happen 😊
What we’ll add now is exactly what you described:

For a fixed path pattern of length m, each step contributes some score; for each start
node we want the best path score, computed backwards over layers.
Concretely, we’ll:
1. Add a new constraint type PATH_STEP that:
Looks at neighbors via a given property,
Checks their type,
Adds a local contribution (1 if matched, 0 otherwise) plus the child’s path score,
Then takes the max over neighbors → “best path”.
2. Add a CONST_ZERO node type as the base case.
3. Build a tiny multi-hop example where:
Node 0 has a perfect 2-step path → score 2.0,
Node 1 only matches the first step → score 1.0,
Others → 0.
You can later normalize by path length if you want scores in [0,1].

1️⃣ Update constraints.py : add PATH_STEP + CONST_ZERO
Open src/constraints.py and:

1.1 Extend ConstraintType
Add two new entries:

from enum import Enum, auto
from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import List, Optional

class ConstraintType(Enum):
ATOMIC_CLASS = auto()
EXISTS_RESTRICTION = auto()
INTERSECTION = auto()
UNION = auto()
PATH_STEP = auto()
# new: for multi-hop typed path steps
CONST_ZERO = auto() # new: constant 0 leaf for path scoring

1.2 Make sure ConstraintNode has what we need
Your ConstraintNode should already look something like:

class IntersectionAgg(Enum):
MIN = "min"
MEAN = "mean"

@dataclass
class ConstraintNode:
idx: int
ctype: ConstraintType
class_idx: Optional[int] = None
prop_idx: Optional[int] = None
child_indices: Optional[List[int]] = None
# For INTERSECTION nodes
intersection_agg: Optional[IntersectionAgg] = None

prop_idx : which property bucket (p0, p1, …)

Rather than baking “path length” into PATH_STEP itself, the nicest way is:

For a path of length L, just add a SCALE node with factor 1/L above the path root.
That way:
Every path-based concept can be normalized to [0,1] independently.
Min / max intersections/unions behave well, even when you combine concepts with
