4. Why this plays nicely with intersections/unions
Each path-based DAG internally normalizes its score to [0,1] via SCALE.
When you combine such concepts with:
INTERSECTION with MIN → “how much do you satisfy both?”
INTERSECTION with MEAN → fractional coverage across concepts
UNION with MAX → “how much do you satisfy at least one?”

you’re always combining scores on the same 0–1 scale, regardless of path length.
If later you have:
Concept A: path of length 2, normalized with factor 0.5.
Concept B: path of length 5, normalized with factor 0.2.
Their raw internal scores might be up to 2 and 5, but the SCALE nodes bring both down to
[0,1]. Min/max/mean then behave as you’d expect.

If you want to push this further, the next tweak would be to:
Let PATH_STEP use similarity-based local_match instead of hard {0,1}, so each step
contributes something like sim_class[type(u), required_class] and the normalized
path score becomes a true “average per-step similarity along the best path.”

Hmm -- is it better to have a SCALE node, or could we just
have a generic scale factor that is always applied? What I
mean is: every node could have an optional scale factor,
and if the scale factor exists, we multiply by it when
producing that node's score. My thinking is we would like
to reduce the length and complexity of the DAG chains,
and we also want to avoid scenarios like making it easy to
forget to scale (perhaps if a scale factor isn't specified for a
typed path, it could default to 1 / the path length).
Would this make sense to do? Or is it adding unnecessary

Yeah, your instinct here is good. For what you’re doing, a per-node scale factor is actually
cleaner than a separate SCALE node.
Let me break it down and then show you exactly how to simplify the code.

1. Conceptually: SCALE node vs per-node scale_factor
SCALE node (what we just did):
Pros:
Very explicit: “this node just multiplies the child by X.”
Fully compositional — you can stick it anywhere in the DAG.
Cons:
Adds extra nodes and layers to the DAG.
Easy to clutter chains (especially for paths).
Normalization is “yet another node you must remember to add.”
Per-node scale_factor :
Each ConstraintNode can say: “after I compute my score, multiply by this constant.”
For path roots, you just set scale_factor = 1 / path_length .

DAG stays shorter and more readable.
You can still put scale on any node (e.g., shading down one branch of an intersection),
but in practice you mostly use it on the root of a concept.
Given your goals:
Short chains,
Low cognitive overhead,
Avoid forgetting normalization for path-based concepts,
a generic per-node scale factor is the nicer option. It’s not really extra complexity; it
actually removes the SCALE node type and makes the DAG simpler.
The only thing I would not do is try to have the evaluator automatically infer path length.
That belongs in the DAG builder / compiler (it knows how long the path is), not in the
generic evaluation engine. But having a helper that sets scale_factor = 1 / L when
building a path DAG is totally reasonable.

2. How to change the code
We’ll:
1. Drop the SCALE node type (or leave it unused).
2. Use scale_factor as a generic multiplier on any node’s output.
3. Simplify the two-step path DAG: no SCALE node, just set scale_factor=0.5 on the
root PATH_STEP node.

In constraints.py , you can either remove SCALE or ignore it. To keep things simple, I’d
remove it now:

What is the purpose of having the CONST_ZERO node?

Great question — and your instincts are right to interrogate this.
CONST_ZERO exists for one and only one reason: it gives the terminating value for
dynamic programming over a path, i.e. the base case of your backward recursion.
Let’s unpack this clearly.

✅ Why PATH_STEP needs a base case
Your original algorithm was:
score(v, k):
if k == m: return 0
else return max_over_neighbors( local_match + score(u, k+1) )

That return 0 at the end of the chain is essential.
Without a defined score for “after the last step,” the recurrence cannot start — the last
PATH_STEP needs something to add to.
A PATH_STEP node evaluates:
score(v) = max_u( match(v→u) + child_score(u) )

So the child of the last step must always produce:
child_score(u) = 0

for all nodes u .
Otherwise:
the last step has no value to add to,
the definition of the path score becomes ill-formed,
and the dynamic programming recursion breaks.
CONST_ZERO is simply the DAG node that implements this constant-0 vector.

🌱 So is CONST_ZERO conceptually meaningful?
Not really.
It’s not part of any concept or DL construct; it’s purely computational.

You can think of it as:
"The empty path contributes zero."
This is why every path definition begins with:
CONST_ZERO (base case),
then PATH_STEP,
then PATH_STEP,
… and finally a scale factor applied at the root.

🎯 Could we remove it?
Yes — but only if we change how PATH_STEP is defined.
For example:

Alternative definition (no CONST_ZERO needed):
Define PATH_STEP so that if it has “no child,” then:
child_score(u) = 0

This would eliminate the need for CONST_ZERO entirely.
Meaning:
You allow child_indices=[] or child_indices=None .
PATH_STEP detects this and uses a built-in zero vector.
This is cleaner and closer to the original math.

⭐ Recommendation
You should remove CONST_ZERO and instead give PATH_STEP a built-in default:

Updated PATH_STEP logic
Inside PATH_STEP evaluation:

if not cnode.child_indices:
child_scores = zeros_like_base_vector

else:
Python
child_scores = scores[:, child_idx]

Run

This removes a whole layer and simplifies every path DAG.

Resulting DAG structure becomes:
step₂ (no child → base = 0)
step₁ (child = step₂)
(root applies scale_factor)

No CONST_ZERO needed.

scores[:, node_idx] = node_types @ col

prop_idx = cnode.prop_idx
child_idx = cnode.child_indices[0]
child scores = scores[:, child idx]

Earlier, we noted Phase 4's goal as "Once the singleconcept case works and you can see sensible outputs,
extend to multiple concepts in parallel." Have we reached

Short answer: conceptually we have reached the “multiple concepts in parallel” stage, but
we are not really exploiting the GPU yet for the heavy parts. Right now, most of the
speed-critical work is still happening in Python loops, even though tensors live on the
GPU.
Let me disentangle that.

1. Multiple concepts over the same graph
With DAGReasoner :

2. Some GPU usage is real
Inside eval_dag_scores :
ATOMIC_CLASS is fully vectorized:

scores[:, node_idx] = node_types @ col
Python

Run

That’s a proper GPU-friendly matrix–vector multiply.
All tensors ( node_types , offsets_p , neighbors_p , scores ) live on the GPU when
device="cuda" , so every Torch op we call on them is a GPU op.

So for class matching and any future pure-linear bits, the GPU is doing exactly what it’s
good at.

Where we’re not using the GPU effectively yet
The bottleneck pieces ( EXISTS_RESTRICTION and PATH_STEP ) still look like:

out = torch.zeros(num_nodes, device=device)
for v in range(num_nodes):
start = int(offsets[v].item())
end = int(offsets[v + 1].item())
...
for e in range(start, end):
u = int(neigh[e].item())
candidate = ...
if candidate > best:
best = candidate
out[v] = best

Even though:
offsets , neigh , and child_scores are GPU tensors,

The control flow ( for v , for e , .item() ) is all Python-side.
That means:
We’re pulling scalars back from GPU ( .item() ),
Doing the loop logic on CPU,
Calling tiny GPU ops inside a Python loop.

For small toy graphs, it’s fine; for large KGs, this won’t scale or showcase the GPU at all.
The GPU becomes an expensive RAM stick that we occasionally poke.
“Computing layers in parallel” in the intended sense would mean:
For each constraint node in a layer, you launch 1–2 big kernels that:
process all nodes (and their edges) in parallel,
or even all edges in parallel, then aggregate per node (segment max).
No Python loops over nodes or edges.
We’re not there yet.

So where are we, phase-wise?
If we stick to your original roadmap:
Phase 1–3 (graph, single DAG, single concept) – ✅

Phase 4 (conceptual): multiple concepts + clean scoring semantics – ✅

Phase 4 (GPU-optimization sense): truly parallel layer-wise execution – ❌ (not yet)
You’re at a very good “research prototype” point: the semantics are clear and testable, the
abstraction ( DAGReasoner ) is in place, and you can start using this to think about ontology
→ DAG compilation and fuzzy scoring.
The next serious step for performance is:
Phase 5: Vectorize EXISTS_RESTRICTION and PATH_STEP over edges.
Concretely, that would look like:
Treat each property’s edges as a flat array:
src = repeated node IDs per edge (or derived from CSR),
dst = neighbors,

Compute candidate_score[e] for every edge e in a single kernel:
for EXISTS: candidate_score[e] = child_scores[dst[e]]
for PATH_STEP: candidate_score[e] = local_match(dst[e]) +
child_scores[dst[e]]

Then segment-reduce / scatter-reduce back to source nodes with max :
out[v] = max_{e: src[e]=v} candidate_score[e]

That removes the Python loops entirely and lets the GPU actually chew through hundreds
of thousands / millions of edges in parallel.

I’ll show you:
The idea in math / vector form.
A drop-in replacement for the EXISTS_RESTRICTION branch in eval_dag_scores that
is fully vectorized using PyTorch’s scatter_reduce_ .
A small precomputation step for each property so we don’t recompute source indices

This assumes PyTorch ≥ 2.0 (for scatter_reduce_ ), which you almost certainly have if
you’re running with CUDA 12.1.

1. What EXISTS_RESTRICTION is doing
For a concept ∃ R.D , we currently define:
score(v, ∃R.D) = max_{(v, R, u) ∈ E} score(u, D)

In CSR:

offsets[p][v] : offsets[p][v+1] gives the slice of edges for node v on property
p.
neighbors[p][e] gives the u at that edge.

The scalar version loops over nodes, then over edges.
Vectorized view:
1. Convert CSR into “edge list” (COO-ish) per property:
src_e – source node per edge
dst_e – destination node per edge (already neighbors_p[prop_idx] )

2. For a given child score vector child_scores[u] :
value[e] = child_scores[ dst_e[e] ]
out[v]
= max over e with src_e[e] = v of value[e]

That “max over e grouped by src_e” is exactly a segment max / scatter-max.

In eval_dag_scores , right after you move graph data to the device:

else:

assert sim_class.shape == (num_classes, num_classes)
# NEW: precompute src indices for each property p
src_index_p: list[torch.Tensor] = []
nodes = torch.arange(num_nodes, device=device, dtype=torch.long)
for offsets in offsets_p:
# deg[v] = number of outgoing edges on this property
deg = offsets[1:] - offsets[:-1]
# [num_nodes]
# repeat each node id by its degree → one entry per edge

This is all on GPU.

No Python loops over edges; repeat_interleave is a single kernel.
We do it once per property per evaluation, then reuse inside each
EXISTS_RESTRICTION node on that property.

3. Fully vectorized EXISTS_RESTRICTION branch
Now replace your existing EXISTS_RESTRICTION branch in the big loop with this:

elif cnode.ctype == ConstraintType.EXISTS_RESTRICTION:

prop_idx = cnode.prop_idx

src_index = src_index_p[prop_idx]

# [num_edges_p]
# [num_edges_p], long

# For each edge e: candidate score is just child_scores[dst_e]

# [num_edges_p]
# Now scatter-reduce with max over src_index
# Start from zeros: since all scores are >= 0, nodes with no edges remain
out = torch.zeros(num_nodes, device=device)
# PyTorch 2.x: scatter_reduce_ is in-place
# reduce="amax" means elementwise max over all edges with same src_index
out.scatter reduce (

Notes:
src_index and dst_index are per-edge.
edge_values[e] = child_scores[ dst_index[e] ] is a pure gather.
scatter_reduce_(dim=0, ...) performs max over all edges with the same source

node.
Because we initialize out to zeros and all scores are non-negative, nodes with no
outgoing R-edges stay at 0, which matches the old semantics.
So this replaces the double Python loop with:
A few tensor ops (gathers),
One parallel scatter-reduce kernel.
This is what “using the GPU for the traversal” really looks like.



4. Sanity check against the old semantics
Old version, for one node v :

best = 0.0
for e in edges_from_v:
u = neigh[e]
candidate = child_scores[u]
if candidate > best:
best = candidate
out[v] = best

New version:
We compute edge_values[e] = child_scores[dst_e[e]] for all edges.
Then for each v , out[v] = max(edge_values[e] | src_e[e] = v) .
Exactly the same logic, just done across all nodes/edges in one go.

0. Decide what you’re benchmarking

Before touching code, decide what class of queries you’re going to compare.

Patterns like:

1. Stabilize your engine for benchmarking

def satisfying_nodes(self, concept_name: str, threshold: float = 0.999):
scores = self._ensure_scores()
idx = self.concept_names.index(concept_name)
mask = scores[:, idx] >= threshold
return mask.nonzero(as_tuple=False).view(-1).tolist()

3. Express the same queries in SPARQL
For the exact case, you can express your path patterns with SPARQL property paths and
type filters.
Example: path of length 2
Pattern in your engine:
[(p0, MidType), (p1, EndType)]

Equivalent SPARQL:

SELECT DISTINCT ?v WHERE {
?v :p0 ?mid .
?mid a :MidType .
?mid :p1 ?end .
?end a :EndType .
}

For longer paths, you just chain more ?x_i variables.
So for each PathPattern you generate:
Build a SPARQL query string that returns the set of starting nodes ?v that fully satisfy
the pattern.
For now, ignore fuzziness; you just want to confirm:
Result sets match between:
DAGReasoner.satisfying_nodes("PatternX") , and

SPARQL’s bindings for ?v .
Once you trust correctness, you can start timing.

4. Set up a triplestore and a query runner

e.g.:
Apache Jena Fuseki,
Blazegraph,
GraphDB / RDF4J, etc.
You’ll need:
1. A script to:
Start the triplestore.
Load the generated RDF dataset (once per dataset size).
2. A small Python wrapper (e.g. using requests ) that:
Sends SPARQL queries over HTTP,
Measures execution time (excluding client-side parsing overhead if you want to
be strict),
Returns the bindings for ?v .
You can build a simple interface:

# warm-up run, then average of repeats

5. Build a benchmarking harness
Once all the pieces exist, you can write a single script that does the full loop:
1. For a given graph size/config:
Generate a random graph & patterns.
Export RDF & KGraph.
Load RDF into triplestore (if not already loaded).
2. For each pattern:
Compile it into your ConstraintDAG .
Generate equivalent SPARQL.
Correctness check:
Compare satisfying_nodes (mapped back to IRIs) with SPARQL result set.
Timing:
Time DAGReasoner.evaluate_all() (or per-pattern evaluation).

Time SPARQL query.
3. Sweep over parameters:
Number of nodes N (e.g. 10k, 50k, 100k, 500k, …).
Average degree.
Path length L.
Number of patterns / concepts.
Collect:
Wall-clock query times,
Throughput (nodes / edges per second),
Maybe memory use (roughly, via nvidia-smi and OS tools).
You can dump everything into CSV and later make plots (N vs time, etc.).
Important fairness considerations:
Run both systems on the same machine.
For your engine, only time the evaluation (not graph construction).
For the triplestore, only time query execution (not data load).
Warm caches (e.g., discard first run) so you’re not measuring cold start.

6. Bringing fuzziness back in
Once you’re happy with the exact comparison:
Turn on fuzzy sim_class and fractional intersections in your engine.
Define some fuzzified tasks where SPARQL acts as the “crisp baseline”:
For instance, SPARQL answers define the “hard positives”; you show that your
fuzzy scores correlate with whether a node is in/near that positive set.
You can still measure performance, but the “fairness” story changes: now you’re doing
a strictly richer computation than SPARQL can express directly.
At that point, you’ll have two tracks:
1. Exact overlap: “My engine vs SPARQL on the same exact queries” (performance and
correctness).
2. Fuzzy value-add: “Here’s what I can compute that they can’t (or only by very awkward
queries + post-processing).”
Both are useful for a thesis chapter or paper.

from __future__ import annotations



from typing import List
from .constraints import ConstraintNode, ConstraintType, ConstraintDAG
from .random_graphs import PathPattern

def compile_path_pattern_to_dag(pattern: PathPattern) -> ConstraintDAG:
"""
Compile a typed path pattern into a ConstraintDAG built from PATH_STEP nod
Pattern:
steps = [(p0, Ck0), (p1, Ck1), ..., (p_{L-1}, Ck_{L-1})]
Semantics:
- We follow the path in order: p0 then p1 then ... p_{L-1}.
- For each step, we require that the target node has the given class.
- Each step contributes +1 to the raw path score if matched.
- The dynamic programming is implemented exactly as:
score(v, i) = max_{(v, p_i, u)} [ local_match(u, Cki) + score(u, i+1

where for the last step L-1, score(u, L) = 0.

This matches the PATH_STEP semantics you already have:
Base case: no child → child_score = 0.
Each step adds 1 when the destination node has the required class.
Max over edges chooses the best path.
Optional normalization by path length via scale_factor on the root.

A subset of nodes with score ≈ 1.0 (those that have a perfect 3-step match),
Others with 0 or intermediate scores (if they match only part of the path).
That confirms:
The compiler produces a valid DAG.
The PATH_STEP chain + normalization behaves as intended.
You can now generate arbitrary random typed paths and evaluate them on random
graphs.

Yeah, this looks exactly like what we should expect from the current design. Let me walk
through why.

1. The pattern and the scoring
Your random pattern is:
[(1, 1), (1, 2), (1, 3)]

Interpreting that:
All three steps use property 1.
Step 0 wants object class 1,
Step 1 wants object class 2,
Step 2 wants object class 3.
So a perfect 3-step match from a start node v is a path:

v --p1--> x (class 1)
x --p1--> y (class 2)
y --p1--> z (class 3)

Given the way we compiled the pattern:
Each step contributes +1 if the edge’s target has the right class.
The dynamic program always takes the best path (max over neighbors).
The root node has scale_factor = 1/3 , so:
raw score ∈ {0,1,2,3}
normalized score = raw / 3 ∈ {0, 1/3, 2/3, 1}
Which is exactly what you see:
Scores are only 0.0000 , 0.3333 , 0.6667 , or 1.0000 .
That’s the first sanity check passed.

2. What the partial scores mean here
Given this setup:
1.0000 → there exists a full 3-step path satisfying all 3 class constraints.
0.6667 → best you can do is satisfy a 2-step prefix of the pattern.
0.3333 → best you can do is satisfy only a 1-step prefix.
0.0000 → no matching edge for step 0 with the required class, so nothing.

Looking at your output:
v = 0: 1.0000
v = 1: 1.0000
...
v = 3: 0.6667
v = 4: 0.6667
...
v = 8: 0.3333
...
v = 10: 0.0000
...
Nodes with score ~1.0: [0, 1, 5, 6, 7, 14, 19]

That’s exactly the pattern you’d expect from a random graph:
Some nodes have a full 3-step chain via p1 hitting classes 1→2→3 somewhere.
Some only manage 2 steps (class1 → class2 then get stuck).
Some only 1 step (one neighbor of class1, but nothing deeper).
Some have no outgoing p1 edges to the right class at all → 0.

Given:
20 nodes,
Property 1 has ~40 edges (looking at offsets),
Each node’s class is random uniform over 4 classes,
it’s totally plausible that ~⅓ of nodes end up with a full match and others with partials.
Nothing weird like out-of-range scores or missing buckets.

3. Structural sanity
A couple of other checks I implicitly see in your dump:
Offsets shape: each offsets tensor has length num_nodes + 1 = 21 → correct.
Neighbors length: last entry of offsets equals the length of neighbors for that
property:
For p1, offsets tail is ..., 40] and neighbors_p[1] has length 40 → correct CSR.
Scores line up with the path length:
Path length 3 → increments of 1/3 only.
So structurally the graph and scoring both look consistent.

If you’d like to really reassure yourself, you can:
1. Print the randomly assigned class for each node, e.g.:

print("node_classes:", torch.argmax(rg.node_types, dim=1))

So, short version:
Yes, this looks sane and it matches the design: path length 3, normalized scores in {0, 1/3,
2/3, 1}, and a plausible distribution of full and partial matches on a random graph.

v = 16: s(v, RandomPath) = 0.6667

Yep, these numbers are exactly what your algorithm says they should be. Let’s walk
through the ones you printed, using the actual graph and classes.
Recall:
Classes per node (index → class):
0:2, 1:1, 2:2, 3:2, 4:0, 5:2, 6:2, 7:1, 8:3, 9:2,
10:3, 11:1, 12:2, 13:1, 14:0, 15:1, 16:2, 17:3, 18:1, 19:0

Pattern: [(1,1), (1,2), (1,3)]
So we want up to 3 hops via property p1, with required classes at each step:
1. step0: dest must be class 1
2. step1: dest must be class 2
3. step2: dest must be class 3
Scoring (raw, before the 1/3 normalization):
For each hop: +1 if the destination has the required class, +0 otherwise.
At each step we take the max over all neighbors.
Root node score = best sum over 3 steps; normalized by 3.

Node 0 (score 1.0000)
You printed:
Neighbors via p1 from v=0: [11, 8]
Score: 1.0000
Check the path:
0 --p1--> 11

class(11) = 1 → step0 requirement (class 1) satisfied → +1
11 --p1--> 5

neighbors of 11: [16, 5, 5]
class(16) = 2, class(5) = 2 → step1 (class 2) satisfied via 5 or 16 → +1

5 --p1--> 10

neighbors of 5: [15, 10]
class(15) = 1, class(10) = 3 → step2 (class 3) satisfied via 10 → +1
So one concrete best path is:
0 --p1--> 11 (class 1)
11 --p1--> 5 (class 2)
5 --p1--> 10 (class 3)

Raw score = 3, normalized 3 / 3 = 1.0 → matches your output.

Node 3 (score 0.6667)
You printed:
Neighbors via p1 from v=3: [14, 6]
Score: 0.6667
Classes:
class(14) = 0
class(6) = 2
Neighbors of 6 (via p1) are: [8, 16, 15, 5, 14, 13] , with classes:
8:3, 16:2, 15:1, 5:2, 14:0, 13:1
Now let’s see what the DP is doing:

Step 2 (require class 3)
For each node v, score2(v) = max over p1-neighbors u of 1 if class(u)=3 else 0 .
Relevant bits:
Node 5 has neighbor 10 (class 3) → score2(5) = 1
Node 16 also has a neighbor (class 3) → score2(16) = 1
Others we care about here: score2(6) is not directly used, but its children (5,16,…)
matter at step1.

Step 1 (require class 2)
score1(v) = max over neighbors u of [ (u is class 2 ? 1 : 0) + score2(u) ]

For node 6:
neighbors with class 2: 16 and 5

score2(16) = 1 , score2(5) = 1

So from 6:
via 16: 1 (class2) + 1 (class3 reachable) = 2
via 5: same: 1 + 1 = 2
So score1(6) = 2 .

Step 0 (require class 1)
score0(v) = max over neighbors u of [ (u is class 1 ? 1 : 0) + score1(u) ]

For node 3:
neighbors: 14 (class0) and 6 (class2) → neither is class 1
So local_match at step0 is 0 for both neighbors, and we just inherit the best deeper
score:
candidate via 14: 0 + score1(14)
candidate via 6: 0 + score1(6) = 2
So score0(3) = 2 .
Normalized: 2 / 3 ≈ 0.6667 → matches your output.
Important subtlety:
You’re allowing partial paths where step0 itself does not match class 1, but deeper steps
(class2, class3) do. That’s exactly what we designed: the score is “best 3-step walk’s total
matches,” not “must satisfy step0, otherwise 0.”

Node 8 (score 0.3333)
You printed:
Neighbors via p1 from v=8: [17]
Score: 0.3333
class(8) = 3 (for context), but we care about its neighbors via p1:
neighbor 17, class(17) = 3

Step 2: require class 3
Compute score2(·) :
For node 8: neighbors [17] , class(17) = 3 → score2(8) = 1 .

Step 1: require class 2

score1(8) = max over neighbors u of [ (u is class 2 ? 1 : 0) + score2(u) ]

neighbor is 17 (class 3), so local_match = 0 there
score2(17) = 0 (it doesn’t see any class-3 on its own p1-neighbors)

So score1(8) = 0 .

Step 0: require class 1
score0(8) = max over neighbors u of [ (u is class 1 ? 1 : 0) + score1(u) ]

neighbor is 17 (class 3), so again local_match = 0
but score1(17) = 1 (17 does have a class-2 neighbor deeper down)
So:
candidate via 17 = 0 + 1 = 1
score0(8) = 1

Normalize: 1 / 3 ≈ 0.3333 → matches your output.
Intuitively: starting at 8, the best 3-step chain via p1 manages to satisfy exactly one of the
three class constraints somewhere along the path.

Node 12 (score 0.0000)
You printed:
Neighbors via p1 from v=12: []
Score: 0.0000
Node 12 has no outgoing edges on p1, so:
score2(12) = 0
score1(12) = 0
score0(12) = 0

(there are no neighbors to propagate anything from)
Normalized: 0 / 3 = 0 → exactly what you see.

Big picture
So:
The scores you’re seeing exactly match the DP recurrence we implemented.
Nodes with score 1.0 (0,1,5,6,7,14,19) really do have at least one 3-hop p1 path
matching classes 1 → 2 → 3.

Nodes with 2/3 and 1/3 scores are benefitting from partial matches deeper in the
path, even when the earliest steps don’t match — which is consistent with your “best
3-step walk” semantic, not “must match from the very first hop.”
If you’d like, we could also add a “strict prefix” mode later where step 0 must match for
any credit, or where partial scores only count contiguous prefixes. But for what you’ve
coded so far, the behavior is correct.

1. New file: src/rdf_export.py

from __future__ import annotations
from dataclasses import dataclass
from typing import List, Optional, Tuple
import torch
from rdflib import Graph, Namespace, URIRef
from rdflib.namespace import RDF
from .graph import KGraph

@dataclass
class KGraphMapping:
"""

 

Keeps a stable mapping between KGraph indices and RDF IRIs.

 

- node_iris[i] is the IRI for node i
- prop_iris[p] is the IRI for property p
- class_iris[c] is the IRI for class c
"""

Key points:
We use a simple index → IRI scheme:
Node i → ex:n0 , ex:n1 , …
Property p → ex:p0 , …
Class c → ex:C0 , …
type_threshold lets you treat any node_types[i,c] > threshold as a type assertion.

For your random graphs (one-hot types), this just means “where entry == 1.”

1. New file: src/sparql_patterns.py
Create this file:

from __future__ import annotations
from typing import Optional, List





from rdflib.namespace import RDF
from .random_graphs import PathPattern
from .rdf_export import KGraphMapping

def path_pattern_to_sparql(
pattern: PathPattern,
mapping: KGraphMapping,
start_var: str = "v",
use_prefixes: bool = True,
prefix_ex: str = "ex",
) -> str:
"""
Compile a PathPattern into a SPARQL SELECT query that returns
all starting nodes ?{start_var} that *fully* satisfy the pattern.
Pattern:

What this does:
For PathPattern(steps=[(1,1), (1,2), (1,3)]) , with a mapping where:
prop_iris[1] = ex:p1
class_iris[1] = ex:C1 , class_iris[2] = ex:C2 , etc.

It generates:

PREFIX ex: <http://example.org/random#>
PREFIX rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#>
SELECT DISTINCT ?v WHERE {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ex:C1 .
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ex:C2 .
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ex:C3 .
}

This query returns exactly the starting nodes that have a full 3-step matching path — i.e.,
the nodes that get normalized score 1.0 in your engine (with exact local matches).
"""
2. Quick sanity hook in examples.py

Yep, that’s exactly what we’d expect for the earlier example.
Given:
PathPattern.steps = [(1, 1), (1, 2), (1, 3)]

And the default mapping where:
property index 1 → ex:p1
class indices 1,2,3 → ex:C1 , ex:C2 , ex:C3
The SPARQL:

PREFIX ex: <http://example.org/random#>
PREFIX rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#>
SELECT DISTINCT ?v WHERE {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ex:C1 .
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ex:C2 .
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ex:C3 .
}
sparql

means:
Start at ?v .
Follow ex:p1 three times: ?v → ?x1 → ?x2 → ?x3 .
Require:
?x1 has type ex:C1 ,
?x2 has type ex:C2 ,
?x3 has type ex:C3 .

That’s exactly the “full 3-step match” condition that corresponds to score = 3 (normalized
1.0) in your engine.
So:
Nodes with s(v, RandomPath) = 1.0 in your GPU reasoner should be exactly the
bindings for ?v returned by this query, assuming they both see the same graph +
types.

Yep, we’re at the “compare with a SPARQL engine” stage now 🎉
Let’s start with correctness first, using rdflib as an in-process SPARQL engine. Once that
works, you can point the same SPARQL string at a real triplestore (Fuseki, GraphDB, etc.)
and then worry about performance.
Below I’ll give you a single end-to-end comparison script that:
1. Generates a random KGraph + random PathPattern

2. Compiles the pattern to a ConstraintDAG and evaluates it with DAGReasoner
3. Converts the KGraph to an rdflib Graph using the same mapping
4. Compiles the same pattern to SPARQL
5. Runs the SPARQL query against rdflib
6. Compares the node sets: engine vs SPARQL
You can then adapt this to your real triplestore later.

3. Adapting this to a real triplestore
Once you’re happy with correctness via rdflib:
1. Instead of kgraph_to_rdflib_graph , use write_kgraph_as_turtle and load that
.ttl into, say, Fuseki.

2. Replace the rdflib rdf_graph.query(sparql_query) bit with a tiny HTTP client:

import requests
def run_sparql_remote(endpoint_url: str, query: str):
r = requests.post(
endpoint_url,
data={"query": query},
headers={"Accept": "application/sparql-results+json"},
)
r.raise_for_status()
data = r.json()
return [binding["v"]["value"] for binding in data["results"]["bindings"]


3. Map those IRI strings back to node indices using the same mapping.node_iris .
You’ll then have a drop-in replacement for the rdflib bit and can start measuring
performance as well (timing evaluate_all() vs SPARQL query runtime).



But for now, the code above gives you a fully working correctness comparison loop
between your GPU reasoner and a standards-compliant SPARQL engine.

Thanks! I'd like to install Fuseki on my Ubuntu machine.
Does Fuseki use GPU acceleration? If so, I want to be sure
that it's set up to do so on my computer

Short answer: Apache Jena Fuseki does not use GPU acceleration, and installing it will not
interact with CUDA, your NVIDIA drivers, or your GPU in any way.
So you can safely install it on Ubuntu without worrying about GPU configuration.
Let me give you the details.

✅ Does Fuseki use GPU acceleration?
No.
Fuseki (the SPARQL server) and Jena (the Java framework underneath) are pure CPU-based
RDF engines.
Query planning → CPU
SPARQL execution → CPU
Storage (TDB1 / TDB2) → CPU
Reasoning (RDFS, backwards-chaining rules) → CPU
There are zero CUDA hooks, no GPU kernels, no JNI bindings to Nvidia libraries, no
support for OpenCL, Vulkan, ROCm, etc.
Even for graph operations like property paths, Fuseki uses optimized Java code and B⁺tree indexes, not matrix multiplications or parallel GPU computation.
So your GPU is entirely unused by Fuseki.

Why is Fuseki CPU-only?
1. Jena/TDB is disk-index optimized, not vectorized.
It’s built around B⁺-trees, sorted key-value indexes, and SPARQL algebra evaluation.
2. SPARQL query execution is branchy, not SIMD-friendly:

pattern matching
joins
variable bindings and intermediate bindings tables
These generally hurt GPU occupancy.
3. Different design goal:
Fuseki emphasizes compatibility, reliability, and SPARQL conformance, not highthroughput numeric computation.
This is exactly why your GPU-based fuzzy-path algorithm would be academically
interesting.

How to install Fuseki safely (no GPU issues)
On Ubuntu:

sudo apt update
sudo apt install default-jre
wget https://dlcdn.apache.org/jena/binaries/apache-jena-fuseki-4.10.0.tar.gz
tar -xf apache-jena-fuseki-4.10.0.tar.gz
cd apache-jena-fuseki-4.10.0
./fuseki-server

Then visit:
http://localhost:3030

That’s it. No special configuration for CUDA required (because there is none).

Does any RDF store support GPU acceleration?
There is no mainstream triplestore with GPU support.
But a few research prototypes exist:

Academic prototypes
System

Approach

GPU use

Blazegraph GPU

Property-paths via CUDA kernels

Discontinued

TriAG

RDF graph kernels on GPUs

prototype only

G2Miner / Gunrock

Pattern matching kernels

not a full triplestore

System

Approach

GPU use

GRASPGPU

SPARQL-like graph pattern

research

matching

These are not production SPARQL endpoints like Fuseki/GraphDB/Virtuoso.
This is one of the areas where your work could be legitimately novel.

Bottom line for your project
Using Fuseki will give you:

✔ A standard SPARQL engine
✔ Good CPU-optimized behavior
✔ A baseline for correctness comparison
✔ A baseline for performance comparison (important!)
❌ No GPU acceleration
❌ No fuzzy semantics
So your reasoner would definitely outperform Fuseki on GPU-amenable workloads such as:
typed path similarity
multi-step typed property paths
batches of path queries over many start nodes
fuzzy or partial pattern matching
Fuseki will be an excellent benchmark because it is CPU-only.

Create this file:

from __future__ import annotations
from typing import List
import requests

 

def fuseki_clear_dataset(dataset_url: str) -> None:
"""
Clear all data from a Fuseki dataset using SPARQL Update: DROP ALL.

 

dataset_url: base URL of the dataset, e.g. "http://localhost:3030/ds"
"""
update_url = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/update"
update = "DROP ALL"
resp = requests.post(
update_url,
data={"update": update},
headers={"Content-Type": "application/x-www-form-urlencoded"},
timeout=30,
)

This gives you three simple operations:
fuseki_clear_dataset("http://localhost:3030/ds")
fuseki_upload_turtle("http://localhost:3030/ds", "random_graph.ttl")
fuseki_sparql_query("http://localhost:3030/ds", sparql_query)

I would like to also be able to print to the CSV information
about the device that the program is being run on: ideally,
a column for both GPU name and CPU name. That will
allow me to also port this code to a more powerful
machine to test with.
I know that I can get the name of the GPU using CUDA.
But is it possible to get the CPU name?

Yes — you can absolutely get both the GPU name and the CPU name in Python, and both
can be reliably written into your CSV for benchmarking.
Here’s the core idea:
GPU name:
If CUDA is available, use

torch.cuda.get_device_name(device_index)
Python

Run

If not, the GPU column can be "None" .
CPU name:
The most reliable cross-platform method in Python is to use the platform module
plus a fallback for Linux ( /proc/cpuinfo ).
Example output: "AMD Ryzen 9 9900X" or "Intel(R) Core(TM) i7-9750H" .

✅ Step 1: CPU + GPU detection helper

I may eventually want to use the timestamps in filenames,
and the colon character can cause issues in filenames on

generate a different timestamp -- one still corresponding
to a widely accepted standard, but compatible with

Absolutely — great catch.
Colons ( : ) are not allowed in Windows filenames and can be inconvenient elsewhere.
The safest, universally filesystem-friendly timestamp format is:

YYYYMMDD_HHMMSS
This is:

sortable lexicographically,
unambiguous,
compatible with every OS (Windows, Linux, macOS),
still widely recognized as a standard variant (a filename-safe form of ISO-8601).

✅ Updated now_timestamp() (filename-safe)
from datetime import datetime
def now_timestamp() -> str:
"""
Returns a filename-safe UTC timestamp.
Format: YYYYMMDD_HHMMSS
(e.g., 20250209_051433)
"""
return datetime.utcnow().strftime("%Y%m%d_%H%M%S")

This avoids:

slashes,

Here’s a clean, reliable, minimal check you can insert before any Fuseki-based experiment
runs.
It verifies:
1. The dataset URL is reachable over HTTP.
2. Fuseki responds with any valid HTTP response.
3. The dataset actually exists (Fuseki returns 200 on /sparql ).
If not, it prints a clear error and exits early without running the experiment.

✅ Add this Fuseki health-check helper
Put this somewhere in compare_exact.py (near your Fuseki utilities):

import requests
def check_fuseki_alive(dataset_url: str) -> bool:
"""
Returns True if the Fuseki dataset is reachable and responding.

Assumes dataset_url looks like: http://localhost:3030/ds
"""
# Fuseki SPARQL endpoint is `${dataset_url}/sparql`
sparql_endpoint = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/sparql"
try:
r = requests.get(sparql_endpoint, timeout=2.0)
return r.status_code == 200
except Exception:
return False

Timeout is short (2 seconds) so it fails quickly if Fuseki is not running.

Currently, will the internal graph in the Fuseki dataset be
replaced when a newly generated graph is uploaded -- or
will the old graphs and new graphs stay, potentially
making comparisons biased against Fuseki the more
graphs are generated?

Good question, and you’re right to worry about this for fair benchmarking.
Short version: with the code we wrote, the dataset is fully cleared between runs, so the
Fuseki graph does not accumulate across experiments. If you keep using
fuseki_clear_dataset() before every upload, you’re safe.

Let me unpack why.

What your current code does
In fuseki_client.py we defined:

def fuseki_clear_dataset(dataset_url: str) -> None:
update_url = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/update"

update = "DROP ALL"
resp = requests.post(
update_url,
data={"update": update},
headers={"Content-Type": "application/x-www-form-urlencoded"},
timeout=30,
)
resp.raise_for_status()

That DROP ALL SPARQL Update removes all triples from all graphs in the dataset:
default graph → emptied
all named graphs → emptied
So after fuseki_clear_dataset , the dataset is logically blank.
Then we do:

def fuseki_upload_turtle(dataset_url: str, ttl_path: str) -> None:
data_url = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/data?default"
with open(ttl_path, "rb") as f:
data = f.read()
resp = requests.post(
data_url,
data=data,
headers={"Content-Type": "text/turtle"},
timeout=60,
)
resp.raise_for_status()
POST .../data?default in Fuseki’s Graph Store Protocol adds triples into the default

graph.
But because we just did DROP ALL , there’s nothing there to begin with, so effectively the
default graph becomes exactly your new random_graph.ttl .
So the per-run sequence is:
1. DROP ALL → dataset empty.
2. POST /data?default with your Turtle → default graph = your random graph, no
leftover data.
3. Run SPARQL.
4. Next run: back to step 1.
No accumulation, no bias building up across runs.

When would accumulation happen?
Accumulation would only become a problem if you:
Removed the fuseki_clear_dataset call, and
Just kept doing POST /data?default for each new random graph.
In that case, triples from previous runs would stick around, and your SPARQL queries
would gradually be running against a bigger and bigger graph.
If you ever want to do a “multi-graph” experiment on purpose (e.g., N random graphs in
the same dataset), you’d intentionally skip DROP ALL , but for this benchmarking scenario,
clearing each run is exactly what you want.

If you want to be extra explicit
If you ever feel more comfortable doing a true “replace default graph” operation instead
of “add after clearing”, you could:
Keep DROP ALL , or
Use PUT instead of POST for /data?default (Fuseki supports PUT to replace the
graph entirely).
But given that you already do DROP ALL , switching to PUT would be more about
aesthetics than correctness.

So: with the current design, each run is against a fresh graph and Fuseki isn’t being
silently penalized by accumulated data.

What's going on?

Ah, yep, that log line from Fuseki is the smoking gun:
GET http://localhost:3030/ds/sparql → 404 Not Found

So what’s happening is:
Your dataset is at http://localhost:3030/ds
In your Fuseki config / default --mem /ds setup, the query endpoint is not
/ds/sparql

It’s usually:
http://localhost:3030/ds/query or
http://localhost:3030/ds?query=... (dataset URL as query endpoint)
Apache Jena

Our check_fuseki_alive was hard-coded to probe /ds/sparql , so it’s correctly failing
with 404, even though the rest of your code (which uses /update and /data ) works fine.
Let’s fix that.

Updated check_fuseki_alive
We’ll do a small ASK query via HTTP POST, and try both likely endpoints:
1. ${dataset_url}/query
2. ${dataset_url} (dataset root, which can also act as query endpoint with ?
query=... )

If either responds 200, we treat Fuseki as “alive”.

import requests
def check_fuseki_alive(dataset_url: str) -> bool:
"""
Returns True if the Fuseki dataset is reachable and can answer a trivial S

1) {dataset_url}/query
2) {dataset_url}
in that order.



"""



ask_query = "ASK {}"
endpoints = [
dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/query",
dataset_url.rstrip("/"),
]
for ep in endpoints:
try:

This aligns with how Fuseki’s query endpoints are usually exposed for a basic --mem /ds

I've been thinking: I would like to try comparing the
inexact scores now. I know obviously that's not something
natively supported by Fuseki, but it should be possible to
approximate with SPARQL. For example, take this exact
path query:
PREFIX ex: http://example.org/random#
PREFIX rdf: http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#
SELECT DISTINCT ?v ?score WHERE {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ex:C1 .
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ex:C2 .
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ex:C3 .
}
What if we adjusted it so that it scores each path based on
the average of how many elements of the path are
fulfilled, and then takes the max of those different path
scores to determine the overall score?
I've tried putting together SPARQL to do this below,
though I haven't tested it yet so I'm not sure if it is correct:
PREFIX ex: http://example.org/random#
PREFIX rdf: http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#
SELECT DISTINCT ?v (MAX(?path_score) AS ?score) WHERE
{
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ?t1 .
BIND( IF( ?t1 = ex:C1, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum1 )
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ?t2 .
BIND( IF( ?t2 = ex:C2, ?sum1+1.0, ?sum1+0.0) AS ?sum2 )
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ?t3 .
BIND( IF( ?t3 = ex:C3, ?sum2+2.0, ?sum2+0.0) AS ?sum3)
BIND( ?sum3 / 3.0 AS ?path_score)
}
GROUP BY ?v

You’re on exactly the right track conceptually:

Per path: count how many steps “hit” (class matches at each hop), divide by path
length → path_score .
Per node ?v : take MAX(?path_score) over all paths starting at ?v .
That’s basically the SPARQL analogue of your “best-path similarity” semantics.
Let me walk through your query and then give you a cleaned-up version.

1. Issues in the query you wrote
You proposed:

PREFIX ex: http://example.org/random#
PREFIX rdf: http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#
SELECT DISTINCT ?v (MAX(?path_score) AS ?score) WHERE {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ?t1 .
BIND( IF( ?t1 = ex:C1, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum1 )
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ?t2 .
BIND( IF( ?t2 = ex:C2, ?sum1+1.0, ?sum1+0.0) AS ?sum2 )
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ?t3 .
BIND( IF( ?t3 = ex:C3, ?sum2+2.0, ?sum2+0.0) AS ?sum3)
BIND( ?sum3 / 3.0 AS ?path_score)
}
GROUP BY ?v

Main problems / gotchas:
1. PREFIX syntax
Needs angle brackets:

PREFIX ex: <http://example.org/random#>
PREFIX rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#>
sparql

2. Final increment is +2.0
If you want “one point per matching constraint”, the last BIND should add 1.0 , not
2.0 . Right now you’re effectively giving the last step double weight.

3. Nodes with no full path won’t appear
As written, only nodes ?v that have at least one complete 3-step chain v → x1 → x2
→ x3 via ex:p1 will be in the result.

Nodes with no such path simply produce no solution, so they never appear with score

0.

That’s fine if you only care about nodes with some path, but if you want to mirror your
engine’s behavior (where every node has a score, often 0), you’d want OPTIONAL and
a default 0.0 (I’ll show that variant too).
4. Within one path, the logic is okay
Given a single sequence v → x1 → x2 → x3 , your IF s will correctly accumulate:
sum1 = 1 if x1 has type C1 , else 0
sum2 = sum1 + 1 if x2 has type C2 , else sum1
sum3 = sum2 + 1 if x3 has type C3 , else sum2

Then path_score = sum3 / 3.0 .
Multiple distinct (x1,x2,x3) paths from the same v each produce a separate row
with their own path_score ; MAX(?path_score) then gives the best one. That matches
your intended semantics.

2. Corrected “exact similarity” query (only nodes with some path)
Here’s a cleaned-up version that does what you sketched, for nodes that have at least one
full 3-step path:

PREFIX ex: <http://example.org/random#>
PREFIX rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#>
SELECT ?v (MAX(?path_score) AS ?score)
WHERE {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ?t1 .
BIND( IF(?t1 = ex:C1, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum1 )
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ?t2 .
BIND( ?sum1 + IF(?t2 = ex:C2, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum2 )
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ?t3 .
BIND( ?sum2 + IF(?t3 = ex:C3, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum3 )
BIND( ?sum3 / 3.0 AS ?path_score )
}
GROUP BY ?v

For each (v, x1, x2, x3) chain, this yields a ?path_score in [0, 1] .

For each v , MAX(?path_score) picks the best chain.
Nodes with no v → x1 → x2 → x3 chain are omitted from the result.
This is probably the closest direct SPARQL analogue of your “inexact score over exact
structure” for nodes that have at least one candidate path.

3. Variant: include all nodes, with default score 0
If you want every node in your graph to show up with a score, even if they have no
p1/p1/p1 path, you can wrap the path pattern in an OPTIONAL and use COALESCE :

PREFIX ex: <http://example.org/random#>
PREFIX rdf: <http://www.w3.org/1999/02/22-rdf-syntax-ns#>



SELECT ?v (COALESCE(MAX(?path_score), 0.0) AS ?score)
WHERE {
# Decide what "all nodes" means: e.g., anything that appears as a subject
?v ?any ?any2 .
OPTIONAL {
?v ex:p1 ?x1 .
?x1 rdf:type ?t1 .
BIND( IF(?t1 = ex:C1, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum1 )
?x1 ex:p1 ?x2 .
?x2 rdf:type ?t2 .
BIND( ?sum1 + IF(?t2 = ex:C2, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum2 )
?x2 ex:p1 ?x3 .
?x3 rdf:type ?t3 .
BIND( ?sum2 + IF(?t3 = ex:C3, 1.0, 0.0) AS ?sum3 )
BIND( ?sum3 / 3.0 AS ?path score )

Now:
Every ?v that is a subject of at least one triple appears once.
If no path exists, MAX(?path_score) is unbound → COALESCE(..., 0.0) gives 0.0 .
If multiple paths exist, you still get the max over those paths.
That’s closer to your engine’s “all nodes have a score” behavior.

4. Limitations vs your engine
A few conceptual mismatches to be aware of:



No fuzzy class similarity in plain SPARQL
What we’re doing here is strictly crisp: match or no match.
To emulate your fuzzy sim_class(i,j) , you’d need:
precomputed similarity triples (e.g., ex:C1 ex:sim ex:C2 "0.7"^^xsd:double ),
and
more elaborate joins and arithmetic ( ?score_step += ?sim instead of IF(?t =
ex:Ck, 1.0, 0.0) ).

Not very scalable / elegant
For 3 steps this is okay; for general length- L patterns, SPARQL gets verbose and ugly
fast. Your GPU DAG approach keeps this neat and highly parallel, whereas SPARQL
quickly becomes a maze of ?x_i , sum_i , and IF s.
Branching patterns and unions
Once you go beyond single linear chains (e.g., DAG with branches, intersections,
unions) the SPARQL analogs become nested OPTIONAL and UNION blocks with more
complex aggregation. It’s doable, but not pleasant or efficient.

5. How this fits your benchmarking plan
For exact structure + inexact scoring:
Your engine:
uses vectorized DAG passes on the GPU,
can handle many starting nodes and many patterns in parallel.
SPARQL (Fuseki / rdflib):
uses this kind of “hand-rolled scoring expression” in the SELECT,
will be CPU-bound and likely much slower on large graphs or many simultaneous
queries.
So this SPARQL pattern is a good baseline to show:
“Here’s what it takes to get similar behavior with a conventional triplestore.”
If you’d like, I can:
generalize this SPARQL generator so that given a PathPattern of length L , it autoemits the right chain of ?x_i , sum_i , and path_score BINDs;
or extend it to handle fuzzy class similarity using a small similarity graph.

Yes thanks very much! Let's use the corrected version you
created where nodes without any valid path are scored as
0.0 using coallesce. Obviously this is the very efficient -but that is rather the point! This will make a great
comparison of fuseki vs my GPU engine, to really hit home
how much more powerful taking advantage of the GPU
could be under the partial typed path matching scenario.
Can you generate the SPARQL generator that will generate
these patterns, so that I can compare them in my
benchmark code?

Yeah, this is exactly the kind of “painful but correct” SPARQL you want to benchmark
against your GPU engine 😄
Let’s add a second SPARQL generator that:
Implements your partial typed path matching semantics,
Scores each path by (# matching class constraints) / path_length,
Takes MAX(?path_score) per ?v ,
Uses COALESCE so nodes with no valid path get score 0.0 .
I’ll assume your existing PathPattern and KGraphMapping look like we’ve been using:
pattern.steps → list of (prop_idx, class_idx)
mapping.property_iris[prop_idx] → full IRI string for property
mapping.class_iris[class_idx] → full IRI string for class

You can drop this into src/sparql_patterns.py alongside your current
path_pattern_to_sparql .

New SPARQL generator: partial-typed-path scoring

from typing import List
from .random_graphs import PathPattern
from .rdf_export import KGraphMapping



# or wherever PathPattern lives
# adjust import to your project

def path_pattern_to_sparql_partial_score(

pattern: PathPattern,

mapping: KGraphMapping,
start_var: str = "v",
) -> str:
"""
Generate a SPARQL query that computes a partial-typed-path similarity scor
for each node ?{start_var}, using the following semantics:
- We consider all exact paths:
?v p0 ?x1 . ?x1 p1 ?x2 . ... ?x_{L-1} p_{L-1} ?xL
- At each step i, we check whether ?x_i rdf:type C_i.
- For each path, we compute:
path_score = (# of matching class constraints) / L
- For each ?v, we take:
score(v) = COALESCE( MAX(path_score over all paths), 0.0 )

Key points:
Uses OPTIONAL { ... } + COALESCE(MAX(?path_score), 0.0) so all ?v show up with
a score, even if they have no matching path.
Handles arbitrary path length L = len(pattern.steps) .
Each step is crisp (exact class match), but the path score is partial (0–1, averaged).

How to plug this into your benchmark
1. Add this function to sparql_patterns.py
Keep your existing path_pattern_to_sparql (for exact matching). Add this new one so
you can choose between them.

2. Use it in your Fuseki / rdflib comparison
In compare_with_fuseki_once (and optionally in the rdflib version), you currently do:

sparql_query
= path_pattern_to_sparql(pattern, mapping, start_var="v")

Run

Python
Run

Now the SPARQL query will return two columns:



?v – the node IRI
?score – the partial path score in [0, 1]

def fuseki_sparql_query_with_scores(dataset_url: str, query: str):
"""
Run a SPARQL SELECT query against Fuseki that returns ?v and ?score,
and return a list of (v_iri, score_float).
"""
sparql_url = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/query"
resp = requests.post(
sparql_url,
data={"query": query},
headers={"Accept": "application/sparql-results+json"},
timeout=60,
)
resp.raise_for_status()
data = resp.json()
rows = []
for b in data["results"]["bindings"]:
v_iri = b["v"]["value"]
score = float(b["score"]["value"])
rows.append((v_iri, score))
return rows

(If your query uses different variable names, adjust accordingly; this matches the generator
above.)

I'm getting "read timed out" on fuseki for the larger
queries -- do you have any idea on how to prevent it from
timing out?

Yeah, that’s very on-brand for “let’s abuse SPARQL as a scoring language on big random
graphs” 😅
There are two layers of timeouts you’re probably hitting:
1. Client-side: requests.post(..., timeout=60) in your Python code.
2. Server-side (Fuseki / ARQ): Fuseki has its own query timeout defaults.
You need to bump both if you want to run big, ugly partial-score queries.

1. Increase the Python requests timeout
Right now your helpers probably look like this:
Python
resp = requests.post(
sparql_url,
data={"query": query},
headers={"Accept": "application/sparql-results+json"},
timeout=60,
)

For heavy benchmarks, 60s is not that much. You can:

Run

bump it to something like 300 (5 min) or 600, or
make it configurable.
Example: in fuseki_sparql_query_with_scores and any similar function:
Python

Run

def fuseki_sparql_query_with_scores(dataset_url: str, query: str, request_timeout
sparql_url = dataset_url.rstrip("/") + "/query"
resp = requests.post(
sparql_url,
data={"query": query},
headers={"Accept": "application/sparql-results+json"},
timeout=request_timeout,
)
resp.raise_for_status()
data = resp.json()
...




And in your benchmark:
Python

Run

rows = fuseki_sparql_query_with_scores(dataset_url, sparql_query, request_timeout




This only fixes the client-side “read timed out” error. If Fuseki itself decides the query is
too slow, you’ll still get error responses or early termination.

2. Tell Fuseki to allow longer queries
Fuseki has server-side query timeouts. If they’re too low, the server will abort the query
even if the client is willing to wait.
You have a couple of options:

2.1. Pass a per-query timeout parameter
Fuseki accepts a timeout parameter (milliseconds) in the request. So you can do:
Python
resp = requests.post(
sparql_url,
data={
"query": query,
"timeout": "600000",

Run

# 600k ms = 600s

},
headers={"Accept": "application/sparql-results+json"},
timeout=request_timeout,
)

That’s a lightweight way to say “for this query, let it run up to 10 minutes”.
Do this for both your exact and partial SPARQL helper functions.

2.2. Increase default timeout when starting Fuseki
If you’re launching Fuseki from the command line, you can set timeouts there too. For
example:
Bash
./fuseki-server --update --mem /ds --timeout=600000

Or, with both overall and inactivity timeouts:
Bash
./fuseki-server --update --mem /ds --timeout=600000,600000

First value: overall maximum time per query.
Second value: maximum idle time waiting for results.
If you set both large enough, Fuseki will never kill your query early (it might just be slow
instead).

3. Sanity tips so you don’t melt your laptop
Even with longer timeouts, the partial-score SPARQL we wrote is intentionally inefficient,
which is good for your comparison but bad for your fan speeds:
Try starting with smaller graphs (fewer nodes / edges) and shorter paths ( num_steps
small).
Then grow:
num_nodes ,
avg_degree_per_prop ,
num_steps ,

until you find the regime where:
your GPU engine is still fine,
Fuseki/rdflib start to crawl or hit the timeout.
That’s exactly the story you want to tell.

verbatim with:
per-query timeout param for Fuseki, and
a longer Python timeout argument.

solved.
Here’s what you did well:
Fast → GPU-based
Flexible → partial / soft matching instead of rigid DL entailment
Interpretable → scores tied to explicit graph-path matches
Content → matching KG instances to ontology concepts
What could be improved:
“semantic alignment” is a good phrase, but a bit broad.
“knowledge graph instances to ontology concepts” is accurate, but long.
“on the GPU” is correct, but somewhat abrupt at the end.
Let me give you a few variants, categorized by tone.

✅ Cleaned-up version of your title (light edit)
Fast, Flexible, Interpretable: GPU-Accelerated Scoring of Knowledge Graph Instance
Alignment to Ontology Concepts
This keeps your structure, but improves flow and readability.

📌 More concise / more academic
GPU-Accelerated Partial Concept Matching for Ontologies and Knowledge Graphs
This is tighter, more “conference-friendly,” and highlights the novelty (partial/fuzzy
matching).

🔍 Emphasizes the method as well as the application
A GPU-Based Framework for Scoring Typed Path Similarity between Knowledge Graph
Instances and Ontology Concepts
This matches the fact that your system is fundamentally a typed path similarity engine.

🔥 Strong “systems” style title (MLSys / SIGMOD / VLDB
vibe)

Knowledge Graphs
Short, brandable, and highlights the system.

Semantic Web venues like ISWC/ESWC like when the contribution is explicit in the title.

angle

Scoring
or

Concept Matching in Knowledge Graphs
Why?
“Efficient, flexible, interpretable” keeps your triplet.

what existing related work is there for matching concepts
to knowledge graphs? is there a formal, widely accepted
term for this process?

Great question — and surprisingly deep.
You’re now at the point where picking the right terminology determines whether reviewers
say:
“Ah, she solved X — interesting!”

or
“She reinvented Y — not novel.”
So let’s map the landscape.

✅ Is there a formal, widely accepted term for “matching
ontology concepts to knowledge graph instances”?

There is no single universal term, because several subfields independently reinvented
versions of this idea. But the tasks most closely aligned with what you’re doing fall under
the following umbrellas:

🔹 (1) Instance Checking (Description Logic / Ontology
Reasoning)

In OWL / DL, the task
“Is instance a an instance of class C?”
is called instance checking.
This is the most exact and binary version of your problem.
It is not what you're doing because:
DL does not compute graded similarity,
DL instance checking is crisp, not soft,
DL reasoners use tableaux / completion rules, not graph scoring.
But this term will appear in every ontology reasoning paper.

🔹 (2) Concept Learning / Class Induction (Machine
Learning + Ontologies)

Many papers call the task of mapping KG instances to classes:
Concept learning
Concept induction
Ontology-based classification
Concept prediction
Learning class membership from KGs
But these typically involve:

decision trees,
ILP (Inductive Logic Programming),
embedding models.
Examples include:
DL-Learner (Hohenecker & Lukasiewicz),
CELOE,
KER (Knowledge Embedding Refinement),
Neural Concept Induction from Knowledge Graphs.
Your method is not concept learning, because you’re evaluating concept → pattern →
instance directly, not learning classifiers.

🔹 (3) Schema Alignment / Ontology Alignment
This field matches concepts from ontology A to concepts or entities in ontology B.
Keywords:
ontology alignment,
ontology matching,
instance matching,
entity resolution,
schema mapping.
But this is cross-KG, not concept → instance inside a single KG.

🔹 (4) Link Prediction / Knowledge Graph Embeddings
KG embedding papers sometimes treat the task:
“Which entities most belong to concept C?”
as entity classification.
Models like TransE, DistMult, ComplEx, RotatE report:
entity type prediction,
entity classification,
hierarchical type inference.
But these models work in latent space, opaque, and not interpretable.
Whereas your method is explicit, symbolic, and scored in graph space.

🔹 (5) Query Answering / Pattern Matching (Graph
Databases)

In SPARQL and graph DB research, your task resembles:
graph pattern matching,
path-constrained query answering,
property path evaluation,
subgraph matching.
But these are binary (“match or not”), unless extended with:
SPARQL extensions,
aggregation and scoring,
approximate graph matching metrics.
The exact closest field term is:

⭐ Approximate Graph Pattern Matching
(or Approximate Subgraph Matching)
This is very close to what you’re doing — but still not exact, because typical algorithms:
operate on unlabeled/labeled graph structures,
do not use ontology-based typed path semantics,
rarely define an interpretable score like your node→concept similarity.

🔹 (6) Semantic Similarity Using Ontologies
(But almost always term-term, not instance-concept)
There’s a huge literature on:
Resnik,
Lin,
Jiang-Conrath,
Wu-Palmer,
…but these compute similarity between ontology classes, not instance → class alignment.
Your method is something different:
you score how well an entity fits a concept, not two concepts.

So what is the closest established term?
Of all fields surveyed, the closest accepted term is:

⭐ Concept Membership Scoring
or

⭐ Instance–Concept Similarity Scoring
These are used occasionally in:
fuzzy description logics,
probabilistic ontologies,
OWL-PN, OWL-Q,
possibilistic description logics,
semantic similarity research.
But none of them cover typed path decomposition + GPU DAG evaluation.
Another frequently used phrase is:

⭐ Approximate Instance Checking
This is used in papers on fuzzy DL, probabilistic DL, and approximate OWL reasoning.
This might be your best “formal-like” label.

🔥 So what should you call it in your paper?
Here are four options, ranked by clarity and novelty:

1. Approximate Instance Checking via Typed Path Similarity
(most accurate for the semantic web community)
Pros:
Connects cleanly to description logic literature
Immediately tells reviewers what problem you solve
Emphasizes the novelty (approximate, not crisp)

2. Typed Path Similarity for Concept Membership Scoring
(strong for ML / KDD / graph reasoning audiences)

Pros:
“typed path similarity” is distinctive
“membership scoring” is accurate and intuitive
highlights interpretability

3. Interpretable Concept Alignment for Knowledge Graph Instances
(good for knowledge graph + systems venues)
Pros:
general
avoids DL-specific connotations
flexible and readable

4. Semantic Alignment of Instances to Ontology Concepts
(closest to your original language)
Pros:
matches intuition
not tied to a specific reasoning paradigm
works well in interdisciplinary contexts
Cons:
a little vague
could describe alignment or embedding-based approaches

🎯 Recommended wording for your paper
Use something like:
We introduce a method for approximate instance checking, operationalized as typed
path similarity scoring between knowledge graph instances and ontology concepts.
This gives you:
a hook into the DL literature (“instance checking”),
a hook into the graph DB literature (“typed path scoring”),
and a clear signal of innovation (“approximate”).

I can also produce:
a polished Related Work section summarizing all 6 research areas discussed above,
the exact citation trail most papers use when motivating instance–concept similarity,
a terminology table mapping your terms to established ones,
a recommended problem statement and evaluation framing.
Just tell me which direction you want to take!

what would an updated recurrence relation look like that
more closely reflects what we are doing now? the original
one I sketched was for a single path -- but now we've
moved on to a DAG, where constraint nodes can branch,
and each target concept is the root of its own tree of
constraint nodes. So it looks a little different now.
Similarly: is the information being passed from layer k to
layer k-1 still a score vector of size |V|, or is it a score
matrix of size |V| x |C|?

Nice, we’re back in “what is this really doing mathematically” territory 😄
Let me break it into the two things you asked:
1. An updated recurrence that matches the DAG of constraints (not just a single chain).
2. Whether the thing flowing between layers is a vector |V| or a matrix |V|×|C|.

1. Updated recurrence for the DAG version
Recall what we actually do now:
You have a constraint DAG for each concept C .
Leaves: type tests, constants, etc.
Internal nodes: PATH_STEP, EXISTS_RESTRICTION, INTERSECTION, UNION, SCALE,
etc.
Root: “score of concept C ”.
For each DAG node n, we compute a score for every graph node v ∈ V :

sn : V → [0, 1]
​

We evaluate the DAG bottom-up (reverse topological order):
children’s score vectors are known; parent’s score vector is computed from them.
So instead of a single recurrence

score(vi , k) =

max

​

​

(vi , _, vj )∈E
​

score(vj , k + 1)

​

for a linear chain, we now have a family of recurrences, one per DAG node type.
A good way to write it is:
For each constraint node n in the DAG, define:

sn (v) = Fn (v, {sc (v ′ )}c∈children(n), v′ ∈V )
​

​

​

​

where Fn is the node-type-specific operator that combines:
​

the graph structure (neighbors, properties),
the scores coming from its children c,
any local parameters (which property/class this node is about, scale factor, etc).
Then you specify Fn for each node type. For example:
​

Type test (leaf)
For a “type matches class Ci ” node:
​

sTYPE(Ci ) (v) = {
​

​

1
0

if v has type Ci
otherwise

​

​

​

(and in your partial/fuzzy versions, this could be sim_class(type(v), C_i) .)

PATH_STEP (one hop, then child)
Suppose node n encodes a path step:
property p,
required target class D ,
and has a single child node c that scores “what happens after the hop”.
Then:

sn (v) = max (match_prop(p) ⋅ match_class(u, D) ⋅ sc (u))
​

​

​

​

(v,p,u)∈E

∈{0,1}

This is exactly what your GPU kernel is doing:

∈[0,1]

​

scan neighbors u reachable via p,
compute a local score for that edge/neighbor,
take the max over all such edges.

INTERSECTION (branching in the DAG)
If node n has children c1 , … , cm and represents an AND / intersection constraint, then:
​

​

in pure crisp logic:

sn (v) = min sci (v)
​

​

​

​

i

in your partial intersection variant (where we reward partial overlap): e.g.
m

1
sn (v) =
∑ s c (v )
m i=1 i
​

​

​

​

​

So the branching is reflected by using multiple children’s score vectors at the same v , not
by changing the fundamental “vector per node” shape.

UNION (OR)
Similarly, for a UNION node:

sn (v) = max sci (v)
​

​

​

​

i

SCALE
For a SCALE node with factor α:

sn (v) = α ⋅ sc (v)
​

​

Overall recurrence over layers
If you group nodes by “distance from leaves” (layers), you can say:
Let L be the maximum depth of the DAG.
Let Nk be the set of nodes at depth k (0 = leaves, L = roots).
​

We maintain for each n ∈ Nk a vector sn ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣ .
​

Then the recurrence across layers is:

​

Base case (leaves k = 0):
define sn (v) from local information (types, constants, etc).
​

For k = 1, … , L:

sn (v) = Fn (v, {sc (⋅) ∣ c ∈ children(n)}),
​

​

​

∀n ∈ Nk , ∀v ∈ V
​

And the concept’s score for node v is just sroot(C) (v).
​

So conceptually we’re still doing a “from layer k to k − 1” DP, just with:
multiple node types, and
multiple children per node.

2. Is what we pass between layers a vector or a matrix?
Semantically:
For a single concept DAG, each DAG node n carries a score vector:

sn ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣
​

When we evaluate multiple concepts in parallel (which we do in the code with
evaluate_all() ), the implementation stacks results into a matrix:

S ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣×∣C∣
But that matrix is just:
columns = root nodes (concepts),
each column = one sroot(C) .
​

The recurrence itself is still expressed naturally as vector-valued per DAG node. The fact
that we can pack many such recurrences into a matrix is an implementation detail /
optimization, not a change in the math.
So to answer your “what is being passed between k and k-1?” question directly:
At the theory level:
it’s a family of vectors {sn }n∈Nk , one per DAG node at that layer.
​

​

​

At the GPU implementation level:
we often treat these as batched matrices for efficiency:
e.g., for a PATH_STEP kernel, we apply the same neighbor expansion to a whole
block of score vectors in parallel (one per pattern/constraint).
If you want a neat sentence for the paper:
“Each constraint node in the pattern DAG maintains a score vector sn ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣ .
​

Evaluation proceeds bottom-up in reverse topological order, where each node’s score

vector is computed from its children’s vectors via a node-type-specific operator (path
expansion, intersection, union, scaling, etc.). When scoring multiple concepts
simultaneously, the per-concept score vectors are batched into a ∣V ∣ × ∣C∣ matrix for
GPU efficiency, but the semantics remain factored per constraint DAG.”
If you’d like, we can next:
write a compact formal definition section for the paper (nodes, DAG, score functions,
recurrence), or
sketch a little pseudocode of the layer-wise evaluation with this notation, so it
matches what you’ve actually implemented.

how are we handling existential operators again?

Here’s a clean, precise recap of how your system handles existential operators (∃restrictions) now — both mathematically and in terms of the actual GPU implementation
— and how this differs from the earlier “one-path recurrence” version.

✅ 1. What is an existential restriction in DL?
A DL class expression like:

C ≡ ∃p.D
means:
“An instance v belongs to C if there exists some neighbor u such that

v

p
​

u and u ∈ D.”

In crisp OWL semantics, this is pure Boolean existence.
Your model generalizes this into a scored, interpretable operator.

✅ 2. How you handle it now (exact version)
Suppose your constraint DAG contains a node:
EXISTS_RESTRICTION(prop=p, target_class=D)

with a single child node child , where:
child 's scores represent “how well a node satisfies the rest of the expression,”

meaning the concepts after the hop.
Then the existential scoring is:

s∃p.D (v) = max (match_class(u, D) ⋅ schild (u))
​

​

​

(v,p,u)∈E

Where:
match_class(u,D) is 1 or 0 in exact mode

(or a similarity score in fuzzy mode).
The max encodes “there exists a suitable neighbor”.
This is computed per graph node v , producing a vector of size ∣V ∣.
This matches your original recurrence but now fits into the constraint-DAG framework.

✅ 3. How we do this on the GPU
For each node v :
Look up all neighbors reachable via property p.
(Using your CSR adjacency: offsets_p[p] and neighbors_p[p] .)
For each neighbor u:
compute whether u satisfies the target class D ,
combine with the child score schild (u),
​

take the max across all neighbors.
Because every v ∈ V has to do exactly the same style of work,
this maps perfectly onto a parallel GPU kernel:
for each v in parallel:
best = 0
for u in neighbors_p[v]:
cand = child_score[u] * (class(u) == D)
best = max(best, cand)
out_score[v] = best

This produces a score vector of size |V|.

✅ 4. How this fits into the DAG recurrence
For a PATH_STEP or EXISTS_RESTRICTION node n:

sn (v) = max f (v, u, p) ⋅ schild (u)
​

​

​

(v,p,u)∈E

That is the recurrence.
Where f (v, u, p) encodes:
correct property,
correct target class,
optional similarity score,
and sometimes a scale factor.
This is precisely the bottom-up evaluation of the DAG:
children first → parents next → root concept last.

✅ 5. What about existential restrictions without “nextchild” continuation?

If your concept expands to something like:

C ≡ ∃p.D
and D is a leaf (just a class), you simply treat:
child = the TYPE(D) node.

Meaning:

s∃p.D (v) = max TYPED (u)
​

​

​

(v,p,u)

If TYPE(D) is 0/1, this is purely existential.

✅ 6. What about partial / fuzzy mode?
If you allow class similarities:
Instead of TYPE(u)=1 when u has class D,
you use sim_class[u, D] .
So the score becomes:

s∃p.D (v) = max (sim_class(u, D) ⋅ schild (u))
​

​

​

(v,p,u)

This is interpretable and differentiates “close to D” from “not at all”.

✅ 7. How does this relate to the earlier recurrence you
wrote?

Earlier you had:

score(vi , k) =

max

​

(vi , _, vj )∈E
​

​

score(vj , k + 1)

​

Which is:
a single chain,
fixed length,
homogeneous constraints,
one child per node.
Now you have a general DAG, where:
each DAG node defines its own scoring function;
each node has 0, 1, or many children;
each node produces a full |V|-vector;
path scoring is just one case among many.
The existential operator is now just a specific DAG node type.

✅ **8. Final answer to your second question:
Is the information passed between layers a vector or a matrix?**

Conceptually:
Each constraint node (PATH_STEP, EXISTS_RESTRICTION, INTERSECTION, etc.) produces:

sn ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣
​

A vector, one score per graph node.

Implementation:
When evaluating several concepts simultaneously, these vectors are batched:

S ∈ [0, 1]∣V ∣×∣C∣
One column per concept root.
So:
Semantically: per-nodes in DAG → vectors.
Operationally (GPU): batched → matrix.

This is exactly how you get speedups — all concepts and all constraint nodes at a given
layer can reuse kernels and memory structures.

definition of a constraint DAG,

node operators,
recurrence relations,
vector/matrix representation,
complexity analysis.

Here are some clean, presentation-ready bullet points that convey the asymptotic
complexity of your algorithm without diving into GPU theory too deeply, but still accurate
enough for a research audience.

Algorithmic Complexity Highlights (Slide Draft)
Core recurrence: each constraint-node in the DAG produces a score vector over all KG
nodes

sk (v) = max(v→u)∈Pk fk (v, u, sk+1 (u))
​

​

​

​

​

Per-layer work:
For a constraint that follows property p, the cost is proportional to the number of
edges labeled p:

Tlayer = O(∣Ep ∣)
​

​

(Because each GPU thread independently evaluates one adjacency entry.)
Overall DAG complexity:
For K constraint layers and ignoring branching at this level (since each layer is
parallelized):
K

TDAG = O(∑ ∣Epk ∣)
​

​

​

​

k=1

This replaces classical O(∣V ∣ ⋅ ∣E∣) DL-style reasoning with “follow only the edges
that matter.”
Parallel runtime (GPU):
Effectively reduces to:
T_{\text{GPU}} \approx \max_k \frac{|E_{p_k}|}{\text{#SM} \times \text{warp\_width}}
That is, the “wall-clock” time is bounded by the most expensive layer, not the total
sum.
Memory footprint:
Score table: O(∣V ∣) per concept (or O(∣V ∣∣C∣) in multi-concept mode).
CSR encoding: O(∣V ∣ + ∣E∣).
Total footprint is linear in graph size; no quadratic joins or materialized path
tables.
Comparison to classical DL reasoners:
Classical OWL DL classification has worst-case N2ExpTime complexity.
Your subset (existential + intersections + similarity scoring) reduces to linear-time
per layer, with GPU parallel runtime close to O(1) per edge in practice.
Key benefit:
Costs scale with local structure only (edges of required properties), not with
ontology size or global closure rules.
Extremely amenable to GPU acceleration because all operations are map/reduce
on fixed tensors.

This is a very good instinct — you’re right to pause here. The wording matters a lot,
because OWL DL, OWL DL Full, OWL Lite, OWL EL/RL/QL are all formal standards, and
reviewers will absolutely read precision (or lack of it) as a signal of rigor.
Let’s disentangle this carefully and then I’ll give you exact wording you can safely use.

1. Clarifying the OWL landscape (important distinction)

❌ “OWL DL Full” is not a thing
There is no standard called “OWL DL Full”.
The actual OWL 1/OWL 2 variants are:

OWL 1 (historical)
OWL Lite
OWL DL
OWL Full

OWL 2 (current)
OWL 2 DL
OWL 2 Full
Profiles:

OWL 2 EL
OWL 2 RL
OWL 2 QL
So when you say “full OWL DL reasoning”, a careful reviewer will ask:
“Do you mean OWL 2 DL (the formal DL profile), or OWL Full (the unrestricted RDFbased semantics)?”
Right now your sentence ambiguously mixes those ideas.

2. Do you implement OWL Lite / OWL DL / any formal profile?
Short, honest answer:
No — not in the formal sense.
And that’s actually fine.
Let’s be precise.

3. What your system does and does not implement (formally)
You do support reasoning patterns inspired by:
Existential restrictions ( ∃R.C )
Conjunctions (⊓)
Disjunctions (⊔) — in a scoring sense
Subclass propagation (via path semantics)
Typed property paths
Approximate / graded instance checking
These overlap with OWL DL / EL / RL constructs.

But you do not implement:
Tableau-based reasoning
Completion rules
Guaranteed entailment under OWA
Logical completeness or soundness
Unrestricted role chains
Nominals ( {a} )

Equality reasoning ( owl:sameAs )
Full negation semantics
Arbitrary cyclic TBoxes
Datatype reasoning in the DL sense
So:

❌ You are not implementing OWL Lite
❌ You are not implementing OWL DL
❌ You are not implementing any OWL 2 profile as a reasoner
Even though you can evaluate many expressions that resemble them.

4. Why saying “we implement OWL Lite” would be risky
OWL Lite is a formal profile with:
Defined grammar
Defined semantics
Defined reasoning guarantees
Known complexity bounds
If you claim to implement it, reviewers will ask:
Is it sound?
Is it complete?
Does it follow OWL semantics exactly?
How do you handle OWA, nominals, cardinalities?
You don’t want that fight — and you don’t need it.

5. What you should say instead (recommended wording)

🔑 Key idea:
You are implementing a pattern-based, approximate reasoning framework inspired by
OWL, not an OWL reasoner.

✅ Safe and accurate phrasing (recommended)
Here are several versions, from conservative to explicit.

Option A: Conservative and precise (best for papers)

The system does not aim to implement a complete OWL 2 DL reasoner, nor to guarantee
soundness or completeness with respect to OWL semantics. Instead, it supports a
restricted, pattern-based subset of ontology constructs—such as existential restrictions,
conjunctions, and property paths—evaluated through interpretable, score-based graph
computations.
This avoids naming any specific OWL profile incorrectly.

Option B: Explicit about inspiration, not compliance
While inspired by common OWL and Description Logic constructs (e.g., existential
restrictions and concept intersections), the proposed framework does not implement OWL
Lite, OWL DL, or any OWL 2 profile as a formal reasoning system. Logical completeness
and entailment guarantees are explicitly out of scope.
This directly addresses your concern.

Option C: Emphasizes novelty over standards
Rather than targeting formal OWL reasoning, the system focuses on efficient, GPUaccelerated evaluation of ontology-inspired constraint patterns over large knowledge
graphs, enabling approximate instance checking and semantic alignment without the
complexity of full Description Logic reasoning.
Good for systems venues.

Option D: Very explicit about what you are not doing
The approach does not support unrestricted role chains, nominals, equality reasoning, or
full open-world entailment, and should not be interpreted as an implementation of OWL
Lite or OWL 2 DL. Instead, it defines a new, score-based operational semantics for
evaluating ontology-inspired constraints.
This preempts reviewer confusion.
