The system does not aim to implement full OWL DL reasoning, including unrestricted role
chains, arbitrary nominals, or full identity reasoning, and it does not aim to guarantee
logical completeness.

This is accurate, defensible, and reviewer-safe.

7. One final reassurance (important)
You are not weakening your contribution by not claiming OWL compliance.
In fact, you are doing the opposite:
You are carving out a new design point:
interpretable,
approximate,
GPU-friendly,
scalable,
ontology-informed,
but not logic-complete.
That’s a strength — and the wording should reflect that clarity.

Can we say that we guarantee logical *correctness* even if
not logical completeness -- in the sense that, if the KG was
logically valid beforehand, it will remain logically valid
afterwards (in exact mode)? I know that formally that
would require a proof, but I think it is a true statement
(though I may not be wording it correctly).

Specifically: if our system says a KG instance could be of
type C, that means that it meets all of the necessary
conditions to be a C even if it doesn't meet all the
sufficient conditions (it wouldn't: OWA aside, saying c
subclassOf c2 doesn't indicate x type c must be x type c2,
but it does suggest it wouldn't be inconsistent if you
added the x type c2 statement in there. We would need to
support equivalentClass specifically in order to account for
nec+suf conditions in the way e.g. pellet reasoner does)

This is a really important subtlety, and you’re thinking about it in exactly the right way. The
short answer is:
Yes, you can say something along these lines — but not using the word “logical
correctness” in the standard DL sense.
What you can safely and accurately claim is a form of soundness with respect to noncontradiction / conservative extension, not entailment.
Let me unpack this carefully and then give you wording you can actually put on a slide or
in a paper without getting into trouble.

1. Why “logical correctness” is dangerous wording
In logic / DL / OWL, the word correctness is usually interpreted as:
Sound and complete with respect to a formal semantics.
If you say “we guarantee logical correctness,” many reviewers will silently read:
“This system produces only entailed conclusions under OWL semantics.”
That is stronger than what you want and not what you mean.
You are not guaranteeing:
logical entailment,
completeness,
equivalence to a tableau reasoner.
So we need a different term.

2. What you actually guarantee (and this is valuable)
What you are describing is closer to:

🔹 Conservative soundness
🔹 Non-contradictory inference
🔹 Consistency-preserving classification
🔹 Necessary-condition soundness
Let’s restate your intuition formally.

3. What your system guarantees in exact mode
In exact mode (no similarity, no fuzziness):
When your system assigns a non-zero score to
“instance x aligns with concept C ”
what that really means is:
All constraints explicitly represented in the concept’s pattern are satisfied by x.
Crucially:
These constraints correspond to necessary conditions of C , not sufficient ones.
Therefore, asserting x rdf:type C would not introduce a contradiction, assuming
the KG was consistent beforehand and no disjointness axioms are violated.
This is not entailment — but it is logically safe.
This is exactly the distinction between:
“x is provably a C” (entailment), and
“x could be a C without violating any known constraints” (conservative extension).
You are computing the second.

4. How this relates to OWL semantics (your example)
You wrote:
saying c subclassOf c2 doesn't indicate x type c must be x type c2, but it does suggest
it wouldn't be inconsistent if you added the x type c2 statement in there

This is spot on.
More generally:
In OWL, lack of entailment ≠ contradiction
Under OWA, adding a type assertion is allowed unless it violates:
disjointness,
explicit negation,
cardinality constraints,
or other hard constraints.
Your engine checks positive structural requirements only.
So the guarantee you have is:
If the system reports a positive match for x against C , then asserting x : C is
consistent with all constraints explicitly modeled in the pattern.
That is a real and useful guarantee.

5. The right formal framing
The cleanest way to frame this is:

✅ Sound with respect to necessary conditions
or

✅ Consistency-preserving under exact semantics
Not “logically correct” in the DL sense.

6. Suggested wording (paper-safe)
Here are a few variants you can choose from, depending on how formal you want to be.

Option A (Recommended, precise and safe)
In exact mode, the framework is sound with respect to the constraints it evaluates: if an
instance is assigned a positive score for a concept, then it satisfies all explicitly
represented necessary conditions of that concept. Consequently, asserting the
corresponding type would not introduce a logical contradiction, assuming the input
knowledge graph is consistent.

This says exactly what you mean.

Option B (Shorter, slide-friendly)
Exact-mode matches are consistency-preserving: any reported instance–concept match
satisfies all evaluated necessary conditions and can be added to the knowledge graph
without introducing contradictions.
Very good for a presentation slide.

Option C (Explicit contrast with completeness)
While the system does not guarantee logical completeness or entailment, it guarantees
that all exact matches satisfy the evaluated necessary conditions of the target concept,
ensuring non-contradictory type assignments.
This anticipates reviewer objections.

Option D (More DL-flavored)
The approach computes a conservative approximation of instance checking: positive
matches correspond to instances that are admissible members of a concept under the
evaluated constraints, though not necessarily entailed members under OWL semantics.
This will resonate with DL reviewers.

7. What you should not say
Avoid these phrases unless you add heavy qualifiers:

❌ “logically correct”
❌ “OWL-sound”
❌ “sound reasoning” (without qualification)
❌ “entailment-preserving”
Those have very specific meanings.

8. How to summarize this on a slide
Here’s a compact slide-ready bullet:

Exact-mode guarantee:
Matches satisfy all evaluated necessary conditions.
Adding the corresponding type assertions is consistency-preserving, though not
necessarily logically entailed.
That’s a strong, defensible claim — and honestly, one many practical systems don’t have.

help you formalize this as a “necessary-condition soundness” definition, or
help you phrase a theorem-style statement that sounds rigorous without
overclaiming.

can you help me generate a formal proof that, in exact

Thought for 27s
Yes — we can write a formal proof, but we have to be very explicit about the scope of the
claim, because “preserves consistency” can mean different things in OWL/DL.
The clean, defensible theorem you can prove is:
If the input KB is satisfiable, and your exact-mode matcher returns “match” for an
individual a against a concept C , then adding the assertion C(a) preserves
satisfiability — provided that the only constraints on membership in C are exactly
the (positive) constraints your DAG checks.
That’s essentially a soundness / conservative extension result for a restricted DL
fragment (Horn-like, monotone constraints). Below is a proof you can use (and reuse in
the thesis), with assumptions stated precisely.

Theorem (Consistency preservation in exact mode, restricted
fragment)
Setting

Let K = (T , A) be a Description Logic knowledge base with standard first-order
semantics.
Fix a named concept C in T . Assume that T contains membership constraints for C only
of the following (monotone) forms:
1. Necessary-condition axioms

C ⊑ E1 ,

C ⊑ E2 ,

​

​

…,

C ⊑ Er

​

where each Ei is a positive concept expression built using only:
​

atomic concepts A,
conjunction ⊓,
existential restrictions ∃R.E .
(No negation, no disjointness, no universal restrictions, no number restrictions, no
nominals, no equality/inequality.)
2. There are no axioms in T that constrain C using negative information or global
coupling, e.g. no:

C ⊑ ¬D,
C ⊑ ∀R.D,
≤ nR.D, ≥ nR.D,
C ≡ … (equivalence), unless equivalence is treated as two inclusions and both sides
are handled appropriately,

C ⊑ D where D later appears negatively, etc.
DAG correctness assumption
Assume your compiler produces a constraint DAG GC for C such that, in exact mode:
​

Match(a, GC ) = 1

⇒

​

A ⊨ Ei (a) for all i = 1, … , r,
​

i.e., a full match implies the ABox contains (or entails, under simple ABox entailment) the
witnesses needed for each necessary condition Ei . In your current implementation, this is
​

“witnessed by explicit edges/types” (CSR traversal + exact type tests).

Claim
If K is satisfiable and Match(a, GC ) = 1, then the extended KB
​

K′ = (T , A ∪ {C(a)})
is satisfiable.

Proof
Assume K = (T , A) is satisfiable. Then there exists an interpretation I such that:

I⊨T

and

I ⊨ A.

We construct an interpretation I ′ that is a model of K′ .

Step 1: Keep everything the same except the extension of C
′

Let ΔI = ΔI . For every role name R and every concept name D =
 C , set:
′

′

RI = RI ,

DI = DI .

′

Define C I by adding the individual a to C ’s extension:
′

C I = C I ∪ {aI }.
Step 2: Show I ′ ⊨ A ∪ {C(a)}
Since we did not change any interpretation of roles or any concepts other than C , and did
not change the domain or individual mapping, we still have:

I ′ ⊨ A.
′

′

Also, by definition of C I , we have aI ∈ C I , so:

I ′ ⊨ C(a).
Therefore I ′ ⊨ A ∪ {C(a)}.

Step 3: Show I ′ ⊨ T
All axioms in T not mentioning C are still satisfied because I ′ agrees with I on all other
symbols.
It remains to check the axioms constraining membership in C , which by assumption are of
the form:

C ⊑ Ei ,

i = 1, … , r.

​

Such an axiom holds in an interpretation iff:
′

′

C I ⊆ EiI .
​

We need to show this remains true after adding aI to C I .
For every x ∈ C I , we already had x ∈ EiI because I ⊨ T . Since Ei contains no
​

​

occurrence of C (membership constraints are “outgoing” from C ), and I ′ equals I
′

′

on all symbols appearing in Ei , we have EiI = EiI . Thus those x remain in EiI .
​

​

​

​

For the newly added element aI , we use the matcher assumption. Match(a, GC ) =
​

1 implies A ⊨ Ei (a) for all i. Since I ′ ⊨ A, we obtain I ′ ⊨ Ei (a), i.e.:
​

​

′

aI ∈ EiI .
​

Therefore, for each i,
′

′

C I = C I ∪ {aI } ⊆ EiI .
​

So I ′ ⊨ C ⊑ Ei for all i, and hence I ′ ⊨ T .
​

Step 4: Conclude satisfiability
We have shown I ′ ⊨ T and I ′ ⊨ A ∪ {C(a)}, so I ′ is a model of K′ . Therefore, K′ is
satisfiable.

□

What this proof does and does not cover
This proof is strong, but it’s not “OWL 2 DL soundness”. It’s a consistency-preserving
soundness theorem for a monotone fragment.
It breaks (or needs extra conditions) if your TBox includes any of the following constraints
on being a C that your matcher does not check:
Disjointness / negation: C ⊑ ¬D or C ⊓ D ⊑ ⊥.
Universal restrictions: C ⊑ ∀R.D .
Cardinality: C ⊑ (≤ n R.D), etc.
Nominals or sameAs reasoning.
Equivalence where the reverse direction matters: C ≡ E implies also E ⊑ C ; adding

C(a) is safe only if you ensure the C ⊑ E direction constraints are satisfied (your
current story), but completeness/entailment isn’t guaranteed.
So in your talk/paper, I’d describe it like this:
“In exact mode, the engine is consistency-preserving with respect to the checked
necessary-condition constraints (a conservative extension property for monotone DL
fragments).”
That’s rigorous and matches what you actually built.

mode” (EXISTS, AND, OR, subclass propagation, etc.), I can tailor the theorem statement so

it matches your implementation exactly, including a small lemma that “exact score 1
implies satisfaction of the compiled concept expression.”

That fragment explicitly excludes:

It means:
You cannot guarantee that adding inferred type assertions will preserve consistency
unless those features are either absent or treated non-classically.
Your table is about what the DAG engine can evaluate, not about what inferences are
logically safe to materialize.
Those are different questions.

3. Reconciling specific rows you asked about
Let’s go through the ones that look contradictory at first glance.

🔹 Negation / disjointness
You originally wrote:

✅ invert score / penalty
This is still computationally correct.
But the proof requires:
No negative constraints on concept membership, because:
scoring does not ensure absence of conflicting assertions elsewhere in the KG.
So the correct interpretation is:

✅ You can detect or penalize violations
❌ You cannot guarantee consistency if you assert inferred types
This is not a contradiction — it’s a scope difference.

🔹 Universal restrictions (∀R.C)
You earlier argued (correctly) that you can implement this as a min over neighbors.
That corresponds to a weak universal:
“All observed neighbors satisfy C”
This is not classical OWL ∀, which quantifies over all possible neighbors, including
unknown ones.
So:
DAG-compatible? ✅

OWL-sound universal reasoning? ❌

Consistency-preserving if asserted? ❌ unless interpreted weakly
This is exactly why your system is better framed as partial / constraint-based reasoning, not
OWL reasoning.

🔹 Cardinality
This is an important subtlety:
Lower bounds (≥ n) are monotone → safe

Upper bounds (≤ n) are anti-monotone → unsafe
Your engine can compute both, but only the first class satisfies the proof assumptions.
So your intuition was half right, and the fix is to split the row.

🔹 Role composition
You said:
Identify patterns, don’t generate edges.
That’s exactly right — and that’s why:
Pattern detection = DAG-compatible
Edge materialization = breaks the proof
So the proof still holds as long as role composition is observational, not generative.

4. The clean conceptual rule (this is the slide-worthy takeaway)
Here’s the rule that unifies everything:
Exact-mode consistency preservation holds for features whose semantics are
monotone and local.
Monotone: adding facts cannot invalidate prior satisfaction
Local: satisfaction depends only on finite, observed neighborhoods
Your DAG engine is perfect for this class.
Anything that is:
non-monotone,
global,
equality-based,
or closed-world dependent
can still be evaluated, but not asserted with guarantees.

5. How to say this cleanly in a paper or talk
Here is a sentence that resolves the tension cleanly and defensibly:
The framework supports a wide range of ontology-inspired constructs at the level of
pattern evaluation. However, consistency preservation in exact mode is guaranteed only

for monotone, positive constraints (e.g., existential restrictions, conjunctions, and lowerbounded cardinalities). Non-monotone constructs such as negation, disjointness, and
upper cardinalities are supported for detection and scoring, but not for guaranteed
assertion.
That sentence makes everything consistent:
with your implementation,
with the proof,
and with reviewer expectations.

6. Bottom line
✅ Your feature list is still correct as a capability matrix
❌ It should not be read as a soundness guarantee matrix
✅ The proof applies to a clearly identifiable subset
✅ That subset already covers the most useful, scalable cases
✅ This actually strengthens your contribution by being precise

extract a formal definition of the “monotone fragment” you support.

You’re very close, but there’s one subtle correction that will make your mental model
exactly right.
Let me restate it carefully and precisely.

Short answer (corrected)
Yes — but the issue is not that “C1 is not a subclass of C2.”
The issue is that there may exist negative or constraining axioms involving C1 or C2 that
your DAG did not check.
So the safe statement is:
If the DAG assigns node n a positive score for concept C, this means n satisfies all
checked necessary conditions for C.
However, this does not guarantee that asserting n : C is logically consistent unless
no unmodeled negative constraints apply to C.
Now let’s unpack this with your example.

Your scenario, carefully analyzed
You said:
Assume we have concepts C1 and C2, and the statement that C1 is not a subclass of
C2.
Important clarification:
“C1 is not a subclass of C2” by itself is not a constraint in OWL.
It just means no entailment exists:

T 
⊨ C1 ⊑ C2
​

​

That alone causes no logical risk whatsoever.
So this statement alone does not create a consistency problem.

Where the real problem comes from
The issue arises if there exists some constraint you did not model, for example:
Disjointness

C1 ⊓ C2 ⊑ ⊥
​

​

Negative restriction

C1 ⊑ ¬D
​

Upper cardinality
Universal restriction violated by unseen edges

Hidden equivalence
Derived type entailments from other axioms
Your DAG engine does not reason over those unless explicitly encoded.
So the actual risk scenario is:
The DAG says n matches C,
but the TBox contains some negative or global axiom involving C that was not
checked,
and that axiom would be violated if we asserted n : C .

Reframing your example correctly
Here is a corrected version of what you were trying to say:
We may compute that node n satisfies all positive structural constraints of both C1 and
C2.
However, if there exists an unmodeled axiom such as C1 ⊓ C2 ⊑ ⊥ , then asserting
both types could introduce inconsistency — and the DAG cannot detect that unless
disjointness is explicitly represented.
That is exactly right.

Why your intuition still holds (important!)
Your deeper intuition was this:
“We’re checking necessary conditions, not sufficient ones.”
That’s 100% correct, and it leads to the correct interpretation:
DAG match = admissible membership
DL entailment = provable membership
Your system computes admissibility, not entailment.

The correct logical framing (very important)
What your system guarantees (in exact mode):
If a node matches a concept C, then asserting n : C does not violate any of the
constraints that were explicitly checked by the DAG.

What it does not guarantee:
That no other axioms (not represented in the DAG) could make n : C inconsistent.
This is not a weakness — it is the defining property of a conservative, pattern-based
reasoner.

A clean one-sentence summary you can reuse
Here’s a sentence that captures everything correctly and safely:
A positive match indicates that the instance satisfies all evaluated necessary conditions of
the concept; however, logical consistency of the corresponding type assertion is
guaranteed only with respect to those evaluated constraints, and not with respect to
arbitrary unmodeled ontology axioms.
That sentence is bulletproof.

One last key distinction (worth remembering)
Question

Your system answers

“Is n : C entailed?”

❌ No

“Is n : C admissible?”

✅ Yes

“Could n : C be consistent?”

✅ With respect to checked constraints

“Is n : C globally safe under OWL?”

❌ Not guaranteed

Once you internalize that distinction, everything else snaps into place.
If you want, I can help you turn this into a formal definition of admissible membership,
which would be a very nice conceptual contribution in its own right.

can you remind me again how we are storing the CSR
matrix and type information? im writing the section of the

Yep. Here’s the “paper-friendly” description of how we’re storing (A) edges in CSR and (B)
type information, matching what you’ve actually been using in code.

CSR storage for edges
We store the KG as a property-partitioned CSR (one CSR adjacency per predicate /
property).
Let:

∣V ∣ = N nodes
∣P ∣ = M properties
For each property p ∈ P , we store two tensors:
offsets: offsets[p] ∈ ZN +1

neighbors: neighbors[p] ∈ Z∣Ep ∣
​

where Ep = {(s, p, o) ∈ E} is the set of edges with predicate p.
​

CSR invariant:
For any node v , the outgoing neighbors via property p are stored contiguously as:

Nbrp (v) = neighbors[p][offsets[p][v] : offsets[p][v + 1]].
​

So retrieving all p-neighbors of v is a single slice over a contiguous region in GPU
memory.
Types used (as implemented):
offsets[p] : torch.int32 of shape [N+1]
neighbors[p] : torch.int32 of shape [|E_p|]

This is GPU-friendly because:
sequential neighbor reads are coalesced,
the offsets array is small and cached well,
each kernel can parallelize across either nodes or edges.

Type information
We represent rdf:type membership as a node × class matrix:

T ∈ {0, 1}N ×K
where K is the number of classes in your experiment’s class vocabulary.

T [v, c] = 1 means node v is asserted to have type/class c.
T [v, c] = 0 otherwise.
As implemented:
node_types : torch.float32 tensor of shape [num_nodes, num_classes]

values are 0.0 or 1.0 in exact mode
we keep it float because it multiplies cleanly into scoring kernels
(If you later want memory savings, this can be packed into bool / bitsets, but float is
simplest for GPU math and batching.)

Optional class similarity matrix
When running in partial/soft mode, we also pass:

Sclass ∈ [0, 1]K×K
​

ChatGPTwhere S

class [c1 , c2 ] measures similarity between class labels. In your current benchmarks,
​

​

​

you’ve often used:
identity matrix for “exact”
a custom similarity matrix for partial scoring
This lets a type test for class C be implemented as a simple matrix lookup / dot-product
style operation.

Quick “implementation snapshot” wording
If you want a compact paragraph for the paper:
We store the knowledge graph as a predicate-partitioned CSR adjacency: for each
predicate p, an offsets tensor ∈ Z∣V ∣+1 and a neighbors tensor ∈ Z∣Ep ∣ encode outgoing
​

adjacency lists contiguously. Node type assertions are represented as a dense node–class
indicator matrix T ∈ {0, 1}∣V ∣×∣C∣ stored as float tensors for efficient GPU scoring,
optionally combined with a class–class similarity matrix Sclass for partial matching.

thanks! can you provide the main recurrence relation + the

Absolutely — below is a clean, paper-ready LaTeX formulation of:
1. the main recurrence relation (general DAG evaluation),
2. the core constraint-specific recurrences (path steps, existential, intersection/union,
scaling),
3. with notation aligned to what you actually implemented.
I’ll keep it formal but readable, and I’ll flag where it deviates intentionally from classical DL
semantics.

Share

Notation (brief, reusable)
Let:

G = (V , E, P ) be the knowledge graph
∣V ∣ = N
C denote a target concept compiled into a constraint DAG
DC be the DAG with nodes u
children(u) be the children of DAG node u
su (v) ∈ [0, 1] be the score of graph node v at DAG node u
​

​

Each DAG node defines a local aggregation rule.

1. Master recurrence (DAG evaluation)
For each constraint DAG node u, the score vector over all graph nodes is computed as:
LaTeX
\[
s_u(v) =
\mathsf{AGG}_u
\Big(
\{\, s_w(\cdot) \mid w \in \mathrm{children}(u) \,\},
v
\Big),
\qquad \forall v \in V
\]

where:

AGGu is a node-specific aggregation operator,
​

computation proceeds bottom-up in topological order of DC .
This yields one score vector sroot(C) per concept.
​

2. Path step / typed edge constraint
For a constraint of the form

∃p.C ′
the recurrence is:
LaTeX

\[
s_{\exists p.C'}(v)
=
\max_{(v,p,u)\in E}
\Big(
\mathrm{sim}_{\text{prop}}(p,p^*)
\cdot
s_{C'}(u)
\Big)
\]

In exact mode: simprop ∈ {0, 1}
​

This is the core CSR-based neighbor max-pooling step

3. Class/type constraint (leaf)
For a class constraint C applied directly to a node:
LaTeX
\[
s_{\text{type}(C)}(v)
=
\max_{C' \in \mathrm{types}(v)}
\mathrm{sim}_{\text{class}}(C',C)
\]

In exact mode: this reduces to a binary membership test
In partial mode: similarity matrix lookup

4. Intersection (conjunction)
For:

C = C1 ⊓ C2 ⊓ ⋯ ⊓ Ck
​

​

the recurrence is:
LaTeX
\[
s_{\sqcap}(v)
=
\min_{i=1}^{k} \; s_{C_i}(v)
\]

This corresponds to all constraints must be satisfied.

​

5. Union (disjunction)
For:

C = C1 ⊔ C2 ⊔ ⋯ ⊔ Ck
​

​

​

the recurrence is:
LaTeX
\[
s_{\sqcup}(v)
=
\max_{i=1}^{k} \; s_{C_i}(v)
\]

This corresponds to any constraint may be satisfied.

6. Aggregated intersection (partial satisfaction)
For “count-style” or averaged intersections (e.g., Vehicle example):
LaTeX
\[
s_{\text{avg-}\sqcap}(v)
=
\frac{1}{k}
\sum_{i=1}^{k} s_{C_i}(v)
\]

Used when partial satisfaction should be rewarded, rather than strict necessity.

7. Universal-style constraint (weak ∀)
For a weak universal constraint (“no observed violations”):
LaTeX
\[
s_{\forall p.C}(v)
=
\min_{(v,p,u)\in E} s_C(u)
\]

⚠️ Interpreted observationally, not as classical OWL ∀.

8. Cardinality (lower bound)
For a lower-bounded cardinality constraint:
LaTeX
\[
s_{\ge n\,p.C}(v)
=
\min\!\left(
1,\;
\frac{1}{n}
\sum_{(v,p,u)\in E}
\mathbf{1}[\,s_C(u) = 1\,]
\right)
\]

Safe and monotone
Upper bounds are not consistency-preserving

9. Scaling / normalization node
For any DAG node with scale factor αu :
​

LaTeX
\[
s_u(v) \leftarrow \alpha_u \cdot s_u(v)
\]

Used to normalize by path length or constraint depth.

10. Root concept score
The final output score for concept C is:
LaTeX
\[
s(v,C) = s_{\mathrm{root}(C)}(v)
\]

Optionally, an arg-max witness can be retained during max-pooling steps for explanation.

One-sentence summary (paper-friendly)
Concept alignment is computed by evaluating a constraint DAG bottom-up, where each
node performs a local max/min/sum aggregation over CSR-encoded graph
neighborhoods, yielding a per-node alignment score in [0, 1].
If you want, I can:
collapse this into a single boxed algorithm, or
add a short comparison to classical DL entailment recurrences, or
give you a notation-light version suitable for a systems paper.

Yeah. Here’s the clean recap.

1) Environment / Python setup
I do not think we ever set up a formal venv , conda env, requirements.txt , or
pyproject.toml together. From what I remember, we were just writing Python modules

and running them directly.
So:
If you do not see a .venv/ , environment.yml , requirements.txt , or
pyproject.toml , then there probably is no reproducible environment set up yet.

Putting a venv inside the repo is not a good idea. It is machine-specific, large, and
should not be committed.
The standard thing to do now is:
create a local .venv next to the repo,
add .venv/ to .gitignore ,
add a requirements.txt or pyproject.toml .
Given what we built, the likely dependencies are roughly:
torch
rdflib
requests

maybe jupyter if you want notebook support
If jupyter command is not found, that usually means either:

it was installed in some old venv you are not currently in, or
it was never installed globally.
If you want to use notebooks again, after creating a new venv you’d do something like:
Bash
python3 -m venv .venv
source .venv/bin/activate
pip install torch rdflib requests jupyter

Then:
Bash
jupyter notebook

or
Bash
python -m notebook

But unless you specifically want notebooks, you do not need Jupyter for this project. Most
of the code we built was meant to run as normal Python modules.

You’re asking exactly the right strategic question. This is less about what the system can do
and more about what claim you can convincingly defend at ISWC.
I’m going to be very direct, because this is where a lot of good work fails to land papers.

🧭 The core decision
You outlined two directions:

Option A — Narrow, well-defined task
“Typed semantic path matching (partial / approximate), evaluated convincingly.”

Option B — Broad system claim
“GPU reasoner / validation engine / SHACL replacement / fuzzy DL system.”

🔴 My recommendation (strongly):
Go with Option A (narrow + deep), and frame upward, not outward.
Then:
Prove one thing really well
Show it generalizes
Do not claim to replace OWL reasoners or SHACL

❗ Why Option B will likely fail (at ISWC)
If you position this as:
a reasoner,
a SHACL engine,

or a general-purpose DL replacement,
reviewers will ask:
Is it sound?
Is it complete?
What fragment of OWL does it implement?
How does it compare to Pellet / HermiT?
What are the semantics of negation, ∀, cardinality, etc.?
And right now, the honest answers are:

❌ not sound in OWL sense
❌ not complete
❌ not a defined DL fragment
❌ not compatible with OWA semantics
That invites rejection even if your system is excellent.

✅ Why Option A will work (if done well)
You already have something genuinely strong:
A GPU-accelerated, interpretable, approximate typed path matching framework
with clear advantages over SPARQL and DL reasoning for this task.
That is:
novel,
implementable,
measurable,
and not already solved cleanly in the literature.

🎯 The framing that fits ISWC best
Here’s the positioning I would recommend:

🔥 Core claim (what your paper is about)
Efficient, interpretable, approximate instance–concept matching via typed path
constraints
Or more simply:

Fast approximate instance checking using typed path scoring on GPUs

💡 Key insight (this is your “idea”)
Many useful semantic web tasks reduce to:
“checking whether an entity satisfies a structured pattern,”
“where exact logical entailment is either too strict or too expensive.”
Your contribution:
We provide a scalable, interpretable, approximate alternative
that evaluates these patterns efficiently on large graphs.

🧱 Structure of the paper (what will make it work)
1. Problem definition (very important)
Define something like:
Approximate instance checking / semantic alignment
Formally:
Input: KG + concept (as structured constraints)
Output: score s(v, C)
Make this crisp. This is your anchor.

2. Method (you already have this)
DAG formulation
recurrence
GPU execution
CSR layout
scoring semantics
This section is already strong.

3. Guarantees (carefully phrased)
You already worked this out — this is great:

consistency-preserving (under monotone constraints)
interpretable (path witnesses)
not complete
This is a strength, not a weakness.

4. Evaluation (this is where acceptance is decided)
This is the part you must get right.
You need two kinds of evaluation:

(A) Systems evaluation (you mostly have this)
Show:
runtime vs Fuseki / rdflib
scaling with:
|V|
path length
ontology size
You already have excellent data here.

(B) Task-level evaluation (this is the missing piece)
You need one concrete use case where this is clearly useful.
Pick one, not three.

🎯 Best candidates for your work
1. Error detection / validation in noisy KGs
detect entities that “almost match” a concept but fail in some dimension
show:
top-k mismatches
explainability via paths

2. LLM output validation
LLM generates structured KG facts
your system checks:

does this entity behave like a “Vehicle” / “Person” / etc.?
This is very compelling right now

3. Wikidata cleanup / type suggestion
“this entity is probably a X with score 0.8”
show:
ranking quality
partial matches

4. Approximate SHACL-like validation
BUT:
do not claim to replace SHACL
instead say:
“soft constraint validation”

🧠 The right level of ambition
You don’t need to prove everything.
You need to prove:
This is a new, useful primitive that:
scales,
is interpretable,
and solves a real problem better than existing tools.

⚖️ What to avoid (this matters a lot)
Avoid claims like:
“GPU reasoner”
“replacement for OWL reasoners”
“complete reasoning system”
“implements DL fragment X” (unless you formally prove it)
These will trigger defensive reviewers.

🧩 The “middle path” (what you hinted at)
You said:
maybe something in between?
Yes — but carefully:

✅ Do this:
Define a specific task
Show one strong application
Then say:
“This framework can also be applied to X, Y, Z”

❌ Don’t do this:
Try to prove all X, Y, Z in the same paper

🧭 Concrete recommendation for your paper
Title style:
GPU-Accelerated Typed Path Scoring for Approximate Instance Matching in Knowledge
Graphs

Contributions:
1. Formalization of typed path scoring as approximate instance checking
2. DAG-based evaluation framework
3. GPU implementation with linear scaling
4. Empirical comparison vs SPARQL

side note -- what exactly was preventing this approach
from being used for SHACL constraint checking /
validation in exact match mode?

Great question—and it cuts right to the boundary of what you’ve built.
Short answer:
Nothing prevents you from using your system for SHACL-like validation in exact
mode—but it does not implement full SHACL semantics, and that’s what prevents
you from claiming equivalence or replacement.
Let’s make that precise.

🧭 What your system can do (SHACL-like)
In exact mode, your system already supports a large subset of what SHACL does:

✅ Supported naturally
Property existence constraints
SHACL: sh:minCount 1
You: ∃ p.C via max over neighbors

Typed path constraints
SHACL: path + sh:class
You: typed path DAG
Conjunction of constraints
SHACL shapes combine constraints → AND
You: min aggregation
Lower-bound cardinality
SHACL: sh:minCount n
You: count / normalization
Explainability
SHACL: reports violations
You: can show missing/weak paths
So yes:
You can implement a SHACL-style validator using your engine.

❗ What prevents it from being full SHACL validation
There are three fundamental gaps:

1. ❌ Closed World Assumption (CWA)
SHACL:
assumes closed world
if a required property is missing, that’s a violation

Your system:
operates under open-world-like assumptions
absence of evidence ≠ violation
Even in exact mode, your system says:
“no matching edge found” → score 0
but not necessarily “this is logically invalid”

Why this matters:
SHACL validation requires:
“If it’s not there, it fails.”
Your system says:

“If it’s not there, I can’t confirm it.”

2. ❌ Universal constraints (strict ∀)
SHACL:
turtle
sh:path ex:hasPart ;
sh:class ex:Wheel ;

means:
ALL values of hasPart must be Wheels

Your implementation:
LaTeX
\min_{(v,p,u)} s_C(u)

This is only:
“All observed neighbors satisfy C”
But:
SHACL assumes completeness of data
your system does not enforce that
So:
extra unknown edges → SHACL cares
your system ignores

3. ❌ Negation / disjointness / strict violation semantics
SHACL supports:
sh:not
sh:disjoint

strict violation reporting
Your system:
can detect violations (score ↓)

but does not enforce binary failure semantics
This is a philosophical difference:
SHACL

Your system

pass / fail

score

hard constraints

soft constraints

validation

alignment

4. ❌ Target selection / shape scoping
SHACL has:
sh:targetClass
sh:targetNode

complex targeting logic
Your system:
evaluates all nodes uniformly
This is easy to add, but currently not built-in.

🧠 The deeper reason (important insight)
SHACL is fundamentally:
constraint satisfaction under a closed-world model
Your system is:
constraint scoring under an open-world, monotone model
That difference is structural, not incidental.

🧩 So can you still use it for SHACL?
✅ Yes — with a reinterpretation:
Your system becomes:
Soft / approximate SHACL validation

Meaning:
instead of “valid / invalid”
you get:

s(v, Shape) ∈ [0, 1]

💡 How to frame this (if you want to mention it in the
paper)

This is the correct and strong way to say it:
The proposed framework can be viewed as a soft generalization of SHACL validation,
where constraint satisfaction is replaced by a graded alignment score. While the system
does not assume closed-world semantics or enforce strict violation conditions, it enables
scalable, interpretable validation-like reasoning over large graphs.

⚖️ Summary
You CAN:
emulate many SHACL constraints
use it as a validator
detect violations
provide explanations
scale far better than SHACL engines

You CANNOT claim:
full SHACL compliance
strict validation semantics
closed-world correctness

🧭 Practical takeaway
For your ISWC paper:

❌ Don’t position it as “a SHACL engine”
✅ Do say:
“can be used for soft / approximate constraint validation”

map specific SHACL constraints → DAG nodes (table form)
or design a mini SHACL-style evaluation that fits your system perfectly without
overclaiming
This is actually a really strong secondary framing if done carefully.

we had discussed this approach potentially being usable

Yes — we did, and you’re remembering something important.
But the precise statement is:

✅ Your approach can be used under CWA semantics
❌ It does not inherently enforce CWA — you have to adopt it at the interpretation
level

Let me make that distinction very clear, because it’s the key to reconciling everything
we’ve said.

🧠 1. What changes under CWA?
Under Closed World Assumption (CWA):
“If a fact is not present in the graph, it is assumed to be false.”
That means:
Missing edge ⇒ violation
Missing type ⇒ violation
No neighbor ⇒ constraint failure
Under Open World (OWA):
Missing information is unknown, not false

⚙️ 2. What your system actually computes
Your system computes:

s(v, C)
based on:
observed edges
observed types
no inference about missing facts
So by default, it behaves like:
“evaluate constraints over observed data”
This is actually compatible with both OWA and CWA, depending on interpretation.

🔑 3. Why it works naturally under CWA
Here’s the key insight:

Your operators already behave like CWA if interpreted that way

Example: existential constraint
s(v) = max sC (u)
​

​

(v,p,u)

If no neighbors exist:
max over empty set = 0
Under CWA:
“no such edge exists” ⇒ violation
✔️ matches your behavior exactly

Example: conjunction
s(v) = min si (v)
​

i

any missing requirement ⇒ 0

✔️ matches SHACL-style validation

Example: universal (your weak ∀)

s(v) = min sC (u)
​

​

(v,p,u)

Under CWA:
if any neighbor violates ⇒ fail
if no neighbors exist:
depends on convention (often vacuously true)
This is very close to SHACL semantics.

🧩 4. So what was the earlier caveat?
Earlier I said:
“You’re not doing SHACL because you don’t enforce CWA”
That was slightly imprecise.
More accurate:
You don’t explicitly model CWA — but your execution model is compatible with CWA if
the input graph is treated as complete.

⚠️ 5. The real limitation (this is the important part)
Even under CWA, there are still differences from full SHACL:

(A) Explicit negation / disjointness

can say “must NOT have property X”
Your system:
can detect absence/presence
but doesn’t enforce global logical negation

(B) Shape targeting semantics
SHACL:
applies constraints to specific node sets

You:
evaluate all nodes uniformly
(easy to fix, but currently separate)

(C) Strict pass/fail vs score
SHACL:
binary validation
You:
continuous scoring
But this is actually a feature, not a limitation.

This is what you should say in your paper:
When the input knowledge graph is interpreted under a closed-world assumption, the
proposed framework can be used to perform constraint validation analogous to SHACL,
with constraint satisfaction computed via exact scoring. In this setting, missing edges or
types are treated as violations, and exact-mode scores correspond to constraint

🔥 7. Even stronger (and very publishable framing)
You can actually say something like:

That’s a strong but defensible claim.

🧠 8. Key intuition (remember this)
Your system = constraint evaluator
OWA vs CWA = interpretation layer

Yes — your approach can absolutely be used under CWA semantics, and in that setting

the execution model, but that you do not implement the full SHACL language or its
complete logical semantics.

We proposed a method of extending this approach to
cover explicit negation and disjointness, right?

Yes — we did discuss a way to support negation/disjointness, but the key nuance (and
this is important for your paper) is:

✅ You can evaluate negation and disjointness
❌ You cannot keep the same consistency guarantees when you include them
Let me reconstruct exactly what we proposed and how it fits into the current system.

🧠 1. What we proposed for negation
The idea was essentially:

Negation as score inversion
For a constraint C , define:

s¬C (v) = 1 − sC (v)
​

So:

​

perfect match to C → 0
perfect non-match → 1
This is:
simple
GPU-friendly
compositional in the DAG

🧠 2. Disjointness via negation
If you have:

C1 ⊓ C2 ⊑ ⊥
​

​

you can encode:

svalid (v) = 1 − min(sC1 (v), sC2 (v))
​

​

​

Or equivalently:
penalize overlap between disjoint concepts

🧠 3. What this means semantically
This gives you:
A soft violation signal, not a logical prohibition
So instead of:
SHACL: “this node is invalid”
OWL: “this node causes inconsistency”
You get:
“this node violates constraints with score X”

⚠️ 4. Why this breaks the earlier proof
The consistency-preservation proof relied on:
Monotonicity
i.e., adding facts doesn’t invalidate previous satisfaction.
Negation is non-monotone:

​

​

adding a fact can invalidate a previously valid assignment
Example:
initially: sC (v) = 0 → s¬C (v) = 1
​

​

later: we add evidence for C
now: s¬C (v) = 0
​

So:
You can no longer guarantee that adding C(v) preserves consistency

🧩 5. So what did we actually “support”?
We supported:

✅ Computational support
negation nodes in the DAG
disjointness detection
violation scoring

❌ Logical guarantees
no longer consistency-preserving
no longer monotone
no longer tied to DL semantics

🎯 6. The correct way to describe this in your paper
This is the safe and strong wording:
The framework can be extended to support negation and disjointness via score inversion
and penalty-based aggregation. However, these operators introduce non-monotonicity,
and thus the consistency-preserving guarantees of the exact-mode semantics apply only
to the positive (monotone) fragment.

🔥 7. Why this is still a strength (not a weakness)
This actually gives you something interesting:
Mode

Properties

Positive fragment

consistent, monotone, safe

Mode

Properties

With negation

expressive, diagnostic, violation-aware

So you can say:
The system provides a dual capability:
safe reasoning (positive constraints)
diagnostic reasoning (with negation)
That’s a nice story.

🧠 8. Important conceptual takeaway
Your system is not:
“a fuzzy DL reasoner”
It is:
a compositional constraint scoring system
That’s why negation works computationally but not logically.

🏁 Final answer
Yes — we proposed a way to support negation and disjointness using score inversion
and penalty functions. However, these extensions break monotonicity and therefore
invalidate the earlier consistency-preservation guarantees. They should be treated as
diagnostic or scoring mechanisms rather than logically sound reasoning operators.

could we prove correctness under a CWA if we allow
continuous computation -- i.e., you only stop once scores
haven't changed between runs? i think i remember a
conversation we had about this -- and it was guaranteed
to terminate as well. This was because it used separate
scores for CWA / OWA, and there were rules about which

trying to remember what the fundamental constraints of
the approach as a whole were, and whether there was

Yes — I think what you’re remembering is basically right, and it’s a useful way to think
about the broader approach.
The short answer is:
Yes, in principle, you could recover a kind of correctness under a closed-world
semantics by moving from one-shot DAG evaluation to an iterative fixpoint
computation over a finite state space.
But that would be a different system from what you’ve implemented so far, and the
proof would be for a restricted SHACL-like / rule-like fragment, not for arbitrary
SHACL or OWL.
Here’s the clean version.

What we were talking about before
We had sketched a scheme where, instead of a single score s(v, C), each node–concept
pair carries a state encoding something like:
whether v is currently believed to satisfy C ,
whether that judgment is “open” or “closed,”
and possibly whether there is an explicit violation.
The simplest form was the two-bit idea you proposed, something like:

[0, 0]: unknown / not yet supported
[1, 0]: supported under closed-world assumptions
[0, 1]: explicitly ruled out / contradiction / violation

[1, 1]: strongly supported / exact / stable
The exact labels can vary, but the important part is that:
each (v, C) lives in a finite lattice of states,
update rules are monotone in that lattice,
and the system iterates until no state changes.
That is the reason termination was plausible.

Why termination was guaranteed
If you define:
a finite set of possible states for each (v, C),
and only allow updates that move “upward” in a partial order,
then the process must terminate, because:
there are only finitely many node–concept pairs,
each pair can only change state finitely many times,
and no rule is allowed to move backward.
This is the standard fixpoint argument.
Formally, if:

V is finite,
C is finite,
each (v, C) has state in a finite lattice L,
and your update operator

F : L∣V ∣×∣C∣ → L∣V ∣×∣C∣
is monotone,
then repeated application

X (0) ⊑ X (1) ⊑ X (2) ⊑ ⋯
must stabilize at a least fixpoint after finitely many steps.
So yes: termination was not the problem.

What this would buy you
This kind of iterative semantics is exactly what you would want if you were trying to
support:
closed-world validation,
recursive propagation,
negative constraints,

disjointness,
“supported unless contradicted” style reasoning.
In other words, it is much closer to:
SHACL-style validation,
Datalog-like fixpoint reasoning,
stratified rule systems,
than to OWL tableaux.
So if you fully built that version, then yes, your overall approach could be used for a large
class of SHACL-like tasks.

What the fundamental constraints were
The real constraints were not “GPU can’t do it” or “DAGs can never support SHACL.”
The real constraints were:

1. You need a monotone state update system
If negation/disjointness are handled by naive score inversion like

s¬C (v) = 1 − sC (v),
​

​

that is not monotone, so fixpoint iteration can oscillate.
To make negation work in a provable way, you need to represent it as a state transition
system, not as a plain numeric inverse.

2. You need a finite-state semantics
Continuous real-valued scores in [0, 1] are fine for ranking, but they are much harder to
use for a termination/correctness proof unless you quantize or otherwise constrain
updates.
What made the earlier idea workable was that the “CWA/OWA” bits or tiered states formed
a finite domain.

3. You need a restricted language fragment
You could plausibly support a substantial SHACL-like fragment, but not arbitrary features
with easy proofs.
The safe fragment would look something like:
target selection,
conjunction,
disjunction,

path constraints,
class constraints,
lower/upper cardinality,
negation/disjointness,
recursion only if the update system remains monotone.
That is already a lot.

4. You need clear closed-world semantics
SHACL assumes the graph is complete for validation purposes.
Your current engine is compatible with that interpretation, but it does not itself enforce it.
The fixpoint version would need to explicitly say:
absence of evidence counts as failure for required facts.
That is a semantic choice, not a computational limitation.

So could this become a SHACL engine in principle?
I would say:
Yes, in principle, a fully implemented fixpoint version of your approach could
support a substantial SHACL-like validation fragment on the GPU.
What would prevent you from claiming “full SHACL” is not a fundamental issue with the
approach, but the amount of formalization and implementation work required:
define the exact fragment,
define the state lattice,
prove monotonicity of all update rules,
prove termination,
prove correspondence to SHACL semantics for that fragment.
That is a major project, but not obviously impossible.

Why this is different from what you have now
Your current implementation is:
one-pass or layerwise DAG scoring,
mostly acyclic,
mostly positive constraints,
score-valued,
designed for ranking and matching.
The hypothetical SHACL/fixpoint version would be:
iterative until convergence,

finite-state or carefully stratified,
closed-world,
validation-oriented,
with proofs of termination and correctness.
So the approach as a whole is broader than the current prototype.

The clean conclusion
I think the most accurate way to say it is:
The current implementation is not a SHACL validator, but the underlying approach
does not fundamentally preclude SHACL-like validation. In particular, if extended with a
monotone fixpoint semantics over a finite state lattice encoding support and violation
under a closed-world interpretation, the method could support a substantial validation
fragment with guaranteed termination.
That is strong, honest, and technically plausible.
If you want, I can help reconstruct that earlier fixpoint idea more formally as:
a state lattice,
update rules,
and a termination theorem sketch.
